10125{_}650{_}Not that it is a personification, only it just caught my eye in a little extract-book I keep, which is full of quotations from B. and F. in particular, in which authors I can't help thinking there is a greater richness of poetical fancy than in any one, Shakspeare excepted.
10125{_}651{_}Are you acquainted with Massinger?
10125{_}652{_}At a hazard I will trouble you with a passage from a play of his called "A Very Woman."
10125{_}653{_}The lines are spoken by a lover (disguised) to his faithless mistress.
10125{_}654{_}You will remark the fine effect of the double endings.
10125{_}655{_}You will by your ear distinguish the lines, for I write 'em as prose.
10125{_}656{_}"Not far from where my father lives, _a lady_, a neighbor by, blest with as great a _beauty_ as Nature durst bestow without _undoing_, dwelt, and most happily, as I thought then, and blest the house a thousand times she _dwelt_ in.
10125{_}657{_}This beauty, in the blossom of my youth, when my first fire knew no adulterate _incense_, nor I no way to flatter but my _fondness_; in all the bravery my friends could _show me_, in all the faith my innocence could _give me_, in the best language my true tongue could _tell me_, and all the broken sighs my sick heart _lend me_, I sued and served; long did I serve this _lady_, long was my travail, long my trade to _win her_; with all the duty of my soul I SERVED HER."
10125{_}658{_}"Then she must love."
10125{_}659{_}"She did, but never me: she could not _love me_; she would not love, she hated,--more, she _scorned me_; and in so a poor and base a way _abused me_ for all my services, for all my _bounties_, so bold neglects flung on me."
10125{_}660{_}"What out of love, and worthy love, I _gave her_ (shame to her most unworthy mind!
10125{_}661{_}), to fools, to girls, to fiddlers and her boys she flung, all in disdain of me."
10125{_}662{_}One more passage strikes my eye from B. and F.'s "Palamon and Arcite."
10125{_}663{_}One of 'em complains in prison: "This is all our world; we shall know nothing here but one another, hear nothing but the clock that tells us our woes; the vine shall grow, but we shall never see it," etc.
10125{_}664{_}Is not the last circumstance exquisite?
10125{_}665{_}I mean not to lay myself open by saying they exceed Milton, and perhaps Collins in sublimity.
10125{_}666{_}But don't you conceive all poets after Shakspeare yield to 'em in variety of genius?
10125{_}667{_}Massinger treads close on their heels; but you are most probably as well acquainted with his writings as your humble servant.
10125{_}668{_}My quotations, in that case, will only serve to expose my barrenness of matter.
10125{_}669{_}Southey in simplicity and tenderness is excelled decidedly only, I think, by Beaumont and F. in his "Maid's Tragedy," and some parts of "Philaster" in particular, and elsewhere occasionally; and perhaps by Cowper in his "Crazy Kate," and in parts of his translation, such as the speeches of Hecuba and Andromache.
10125{_}670{_}I long to know your opinion of that translation.
10125{_}671{_}The Odyssey especially is surely very Homeric.
10125{_}672{_}What nobler than the appearance of Phoebus at the beginning of the Iliad,--the lines ending with "Dread sounding, bounding on the silver bow!"
10125{_}673{_}I beg you will give me your opinion of the translation; it afforded me high pleasure.
10125{_}674{_}As curious a specimen of translation as ever fell into my hands, is a young man's in our office, of a French novel.
10125{_}675{_}What in the original was literally "amiable delusions of the fancy," he proposed, to render "the fair frauds of the imagination."
10125{_}676{_}I had much trouble in licking the book into any meaning at all.
10125{_}677{_}Yet did the knave clear fifty or sixty pounds by subscription and selling the copyright.
10125{_}678{_}The book itself not a week's work!
10125{_}679{_}To-day's portion of my journalizing epistle has been very dull and poverty-stricken.
10125{_}680{_}I will here end.
10125{_}681{_}_Tuesday night_,  I have been drinking egg-hot and smoking Oronooko (associated circumstances, which ever forcibly recall to my mind our evenings and nights at the "Salutation").
10125{_}682{_}My eyes and brain are heavy and asleep, but my heart is awake; and if words came as ready as ideas, and ideas as feelings, I could say ten hundred kind things.
10125{_}683{_}Coleridge, you know not my supreme happiness at having one on earth (though counties separate us) whom I can call a friend.
10125{_}684{_}Remember you those tender lines of Logan?--    "Our broken friendships we deplore,   And loves of youth that are no more;   No after friendships e'er can raise   Th' endearments of our early days,   And ne'er the heart such fondness prove,   As when we first began to love."
10125{_}685{_}I am writing at random, and half-tipsy, what you may not _equally_ understand, as you will be sober when you read it; but _my_ sober and _my_ half-tipsy hours you are alike a sharer in.
10125{_}686{_}Good night.
10125{_}687{_}"Then up rose our bard, like a prophet in drink,   Craigdoroch, thou'lt soar when creation shall sink."
10125{_}688{_}BURNS.
10125{_}689{_}[1] Coleridge's "Monody" on Chatterton.
10125{_}690{_}V.   TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}691{_}_September_ 27, 1796.
10125{_}692{_}My Dearest Friend,--White, or some of my friends, or the public papers, by this time may have informed you of the terrible calamities that have fallen on our family.
10125{_}693{_}I will only give you the outlines: My poor dear, dearest sister, in a fit of insanity, has been the death of her own mother.
10125{_}694{_}I was at hand only time enough to snatch the knife out of her grasp.
10125{_}695{_}She is at present in a madhouse, from whence I fear she must be moved to an hospital.
10125{_}696{_}God has preserved to me my senses,--I eat, and drink, and sleep, and have my judgment, I believe, very sound.
10125{_}697{_}My poor father was slightly wounded, and I am left to take care of him and my aunt.
10125{_}698{_}Mr, Norris, of the Blue-coat School, has been very kind to us, and we have no other friend; but, thank God, I am very calm and composed, and able to do the best that remains to do.
10125{_}699{_}Write as religious a letter as possible, but no mention of what is gone and done with.
10125{_}700{_}With me "the former things are passed away," and I have something more to do than to feel.
10125{_}701{_}God Almighty have us all in his keeping!
10125{_}702{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}703{_}Mention nothing of poetry.
10125{_}704{_}I have destroyed every vestige of past vanities of that kind.
10125{_}705{_}Do as you please, but if you publish, publish mine (I give free leave) without name or initial, and never send me a book, I charge you.
10125{_}706{_}Your own judgment will convince you not to take any notice of this yet to your dear wife.
10125{_}707{_}You look after your family; I have my reason and strength left to take care of mine.
10125{_}708{_}I charge you, don't think of coming to see me.
10125{_}709{_}Write.
10125{_}710{_}I will not see you, if you come, God Almighty love you and all of us!
10125{_}711{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}712{_}VI.
10125{_}713{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}714{_}_October_ 3, 1796.
10125{_}715{_}My dearest friend,--Your letter was an inestimable treasure to me.
10125{_}716{_}It will be a comfort to you, I know, to know that our prospects are somewhat brighter.
10125{_}717{_}My poor dear, dearest sister, the unhappy and unconscious instrument of the Almighty's judgments on our house, is restored to her senses, to a dreadful sense and recollection of what has past, awful to her mind and impressive (as it must be to the end of life), but tempered with religious resignation and the reasonings of a sound judgment, which in this early stage knows how to distinguish between a deed committed in a transient fit of frenzy, and the terrible guilt of a mother's murder.
10125{_}718{_}I have seen her.
10125{_}719{_}I found her, this morning, calm and serene; far, very, very far, from an indecent, forgetful serenity.
10125{_}720{_}She has a most affectionate and tender concern for what has happened.
10125{_}721{_}Indeed, from the beginning, frightful and hopeless as her disorder seemed, I had confidence enough in her strength of mind and religious principle to look forward to a time when _even she_ might recover tranquillity.
10125{_}722{_}God be praised, Coleridge, wonderful as it is to tell, I have never once been otherwise than collected and calm; even on the dreadful day and in the midst of the terrible scene, I preserved a tranquillity which bystanders may have construed into indifference,--a tranquillity not of despair.
10125{_}723{_}Is it folly or sin in me to say that it was a religious principle that _most_ supported me?
10125{_}724{_}I allow much to other favorable circumstances.
10125{_}725{_}I felt that I had something else to do than to regret.
10125{_}726{_}On that first evening my aunt was lying insensible, to all appearance like one dying; my father with his poor forehead plastered over, from a wound he had received from a daughter dearly loved by him, and who loved him no less dearly; my mother a dead and murdered corpse in the next room,--yet was I wonderfully supported, I dosed not my eyes in sleep that night, but lay without terrors and without despair, I have lost no sleep since, I had been long used not to rest in things of sense,--had endeavored after a comprehension of mind unsatisfied with the "ignorant present time;" and _this_ kept me up.
10125{_}727{_}I had the whole weight of the family thrown on me; for my brother, [1] little disposed (I speak not without tenderness for him) at any time to take care of old age and infirmities, had now, with his bad leg, an exemption from such duties; and I was now left alone.
10125{_}728{_}One little incident may serve to make you understand my way of managing my mind, Within a day or two after the fatal one, we dressed for dinner a tongue which we had had salted for some weeks in the house.
10125{_}729{_}As I sat down, a feeling like remorse struck me: this tongue poor Mary got for me, and can I partake of it now, when she is far away?
10125{_}730{_}A thought occurred and relieved me; if I give in to this way of feeling, there is not a chair, a room, an object in our rooms, that will not awaken the keenest griefs; I must rise above such weaknesses.
10125{_}731{_}I hope this was not want of true feeling.
10125{_}732{_}I did not let this carry me, though, too far.
10125{_}733{_}On the very second day (I date from the day of horrors), as is usual in such cases, there were a matter of twenty people, I do think, supping in our room; they prevailed on me to eat _with them_ (for to eat I never refused).
10125{_}734{_}They were all making merry in the room!
10125{_}735{_}Some had come from friendship, some from busy curiosity, and some from interest.
10125{_}736{_}I was going to partake with them, when my recollection came that my poor dead mother was lying in the next room,--the very next room; a mother who through life wished nothing but her children's welfare.
10125{_}737{_}Indignation, the rage of grief, something like remorse, rushed upon my mind.
10125{_}738{_}In an agony of emotion I found my, way mechanically to the adjoining room, and fell on my knees by the side of her coffin, asking forgiveness of Heaven, and sometimes of her, for forgetting her so soon.
10125{_}739{_}Tranquillity returned, and it was the only violent emotion that mastered me; and I think it did me good.
10125{_}740{_}I mention these things because I hate concealment, and love to give a faithful journal of what passes within me.
10125{_}741{_}Our friends have been very good.
10125{_}742{_}Sam Le Grice, [2] who was then in town, was with me the three or four first days, and was as a brother to me, gave up every hour of his time, to the very hurting of his health and spirits, in constant attendance and humoring my poor father; talked with him, read to him, played at cribbage with him (for so short is the old man's recollection that he was playing at cards, as though nothing had happened, while the coroner's inquest was sitting over the way!).
10125{_}743{_}Samuel wept tenderly when he went away, for his mother wrote him a very severe letter on his loitering so long in town, and he was forced to go.
10125{_}744{_}Mr. Norris, of Christ's Hospital, has been as a father to me, Mrs. Norris as a mother, though we had few claims on them.
10125{_}745{_}A gentleman, brother to my god-mother, from whom we never had right or reason to expect any such assistance, sent my father twenty pounds; and to crown all these God's blessings to our family at such a time, an old lady, a cousin of my father and aunt's, a gentlewoman of fortune, is to take my aunt and make her comfortable for the short remainder of her days.
10125{_}746{_}My aunt is recovered, and as well as ever, and highly pleased at thoughts of going, and has generously given up the interest of her little money (which was formerly paid my father for her board) wholely and solely to my sister's use.
10125{_}747{_}Reckoning this, we have, Daddy and I, for oar two selves and an old maid-servant to look after him when I am out, which will be necessary, L170, or L180 rather, a year, out of which we can spare L50 or L60 at least for Mary while she stays at Islington, where she roust and shall stay during her father's life, for his and her comfort.
10125{_}748{_}I know John will make speeches about it, but she shall not go into an hospital.
10125{_}749{_}The good lady of the madhouse and her daughter, an elegant, sweet-behaved young lady, love her, and are taken with her amazingly; and I know from her own mouth she loves them, and longs to be with them as much.
10125{_}750{_}Poor thing, they say she was but the other morning saying she knew she must go to Bethlem for life; that one of her brothers would have it so, but the other would wish it not, but be obliged to go with the stream; that she had often, as she passed Bethlem, thought it likely, "here it may be my fate to end my days," conscious of a certain flightiness in her poor head oftentimes, and mindful of more than one severe illness of that nature before.
10125{_}751{_}A legacy of L100 which my father will have at Christmas, and this L20 I mentioned before, with what is in the house, will much more than set us clear.
10125{_}752{_}If my father, an old servant-maid, and I can't live, and live comfortably, on L130 or L120 a year, we ought to burn by slow fires; and I almost would, that Mary might not go into an hospital.
10125{_}753{_}Let me not leave one unfavorable impression on your mind respecting my brother.
10125{_}754{_}Since this has happened, he has been very kind and brotherly; but I fear for his mind.
10125{_}755{_}He has taken his ease in the world, and is not fit himself to struggle with difficulties, nor has much accustomed himself to throw himself into their way; and I know his language is already, "Charles, you must take care of yourself, you must not abridge yourself of a single pleasure you have been used to," etc., and in that style of talking.
10125{_}756{_}But you, a necessarian, can respect a difference of mind, and love what _is amiable_ in a character not perfect.
10125{_}757{_}He has been very good, but I fear for his mind.
10125{_}758{_}Thank God, I can unconnect myself with him, and shall manage all my father's moneys in future myself, if I take charge of Daddy, which poor John has not even hinted a wish, at any future time even, to share with me.
10125{_}759{_}The lady at this madhouse assures me that I may dismiss immediately both doctor and apothecary, retaining occasionally a composing draught or so for a while; and there is a less expensive establishment in her house, where she will only not have a room and nurse to herself, for L50 or guineas a year,--the outside would be L60.
10125{_}760{_}You know, by economy, how much more even I shall be able to spare for her comforts.
10125{_}761{_}She will, I fancy, if she stays, make one of the family rather than of the patients; and the old and young ladies I like exceedingly, and she loves dearly; and they, as the saying is, take to her very extraordinarily, if it is extraordinary that people who see my sister should love her.
10125{_}762{_}Of all the people I ever saw in the world, my poor sister was most and thoroughly devoid of the least tincture of selfishness, I will enlarge upon her qualities, poor dear, dearest soul, in a future letter, for my own comfort, for I understand her thoroughly; and if I mistake not, in the most trying situation that a human being can be found in, she will be found (I speak not with sufficient humility, I fear, but humanly and foolishly speaking),--she will be found, I trust, uniformly great and amiable.
10125{_}763{_}God keep her in her present mind, to whom be thanks and praise for all His dispensations to mankind!
10125{_}764{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}765{_}These mentioned good fortunes and change of prospects had almost brought my mind over to the extreme the very opposite to despair.
10125{_}766{_}I was in danger of making myself too happy.
10125{_}767{_}Your letter brought me back to a view of things which I had entertained from the beginning.
10125{_}768{_}I hope (for Mary I can answer)--but I hope that _I_ shall through life never have less recollection, nor a fainter impression, of what has happened than I have now.
10125{_}769{_}'T is not a light thing, nor meant by the Almighty to be received lightly.
10125{_}770{_}I must be serious, circumspect, and deeply religious through life; and by such means may _both_ of us escape madness in future, if it so please the Almighty!
10125{_}771{_}Send me word how it fares with Sara.
10125{_}772{_}I repeat it, your letter was, and will be, an inestimable treasure to me.
10125{_}773{_}You have a view of what my situation demands of me, like my own view, and I trust a just one.
10125{_}774{_}Coleridge, continue to write, but do not forever offend me by talking of sending me cash.
10125{_}775{_}Sincerely and on my soul, we do not want it.
10125{_}776{_}God love you both!
10125{_}777{_}I will write again very soon.
10125{_}778{_}Do you write directly.
10125{_}779{_}[1] John Lamb, the "James Elia" of the essay "My Relations."
10125{_}780{_}[2] A Christ's Hospital schoolfellow.
10125{_}781{_}VII.
10125{_}782{_}TO COLERIDGE,  _October_ 17, 1796.
10125{_}783{_}My dearest friend,--I grieve from my very soul to observe you in your plans of life veering about from this hope to the other, and settling nowhere.
10125{_}784{_}Is it an untoward fatality (speaking humanly) that does this for you,--a stubborn, irresistible concurrence of events,--or lies the fault, as I fear it does, in your own mind?
10125{_}785{_}You seem to be taking up splendid schemes of fortune only to lay them down again; and your fortunes are an _ignis fatuus_ that has been conducting you in thought from Lancaster Court, Strand, to somewhere near Matlock; then jumping across to Dr. Somebody's, whose son's tutor you were likely to be; and would to God the dancing demon _may_ conduct you at last in peace and comfort to the "life and labours of a cottager"!
10125{_}786{_}You see from the above awkward playfulness of fancy that my spirits are not quite depressed.
10125{_}787{_}I should ill deserve God's blessings, which, since the late terrible event, have come down in mercy upon us, if I indulge in regret or querulousness.
10125{_}788{_}Mary continues serene and cheerful.
10125{_}789{_}I have not by me a little letter she wrote to me; for though I see her almost every day, yet we delight to write to one another, for we can scarce see each other but in company with some of the people of the house.
10125{_}790{_}I have not the letter by me, but will quote from memory what she wrote in it: "I have no bad, terrifying dreams.
10125{_}791{_}At midnight, when I happen to awake, the nurse sleeping by the side of me, with the noise of the poor mad people around me, I have no fear.
10125{_}792{_}The spirit of my mother seems to descend and smile upon me, and bid me live to enjoy the life and reason which the Almighty has given me.
10125{_}793{_}I shall see her again in heaven; she will then understand me better.
10125{_}794{_}My grandmother, too, will understand me better, and will then say no more, as she used to do, 'Polly, what are those poor crazy, moythered brains of yours thinking of always?'"
10125{_}795{_}Poor Mary!
10125{_}796{_}my mother indeed _never understood_ her right.
10125{_}797{_}She loved her, as she loved us all, with a mother's love; but in opinion, in feeling and sentiment and disposition, bore so distant a resemblance to her daughter that she never understood her right,--never could believe how much _she_ loved her, but met her caresses, her protestations of filial affection, too frequently with coldness and repulse.
10125{_}798{_}Still, she was a good mother.
10125{_}799{_}God forbid I should think of her but _most_ respectfully, _most_ affectionately.
10125{_}800{_}Yet she would always love my brother above Mary, who was not worthy of one tenth of that affection which Mary had a right to claim.
10125{_}801{_}But it is my sister's gratifying recollection that every act of duty and of love she could pay, every kindness (and I speak true, when I say to the hurting of her health, and most probably in great part to the derangement of her senses) through a long course of infirmities and sickness she could show her, she ever did.
10125{_}802{_}I will some day, as I promised, enlarge to you upon my sister's excellences; 't will seem like exaggeration, but I will do it.
10125{_}803{_}At present, short letters suit my state of mind best.
10125{_}804{_}So take my kindest wishes for your comfort and establishment in life, and for Sara's welfare and comforts with you.
10125{_}805{_}God love you; God love us all!
10125{_}806{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}807{_}VIII.
10125{_}808{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}809{_}_November_ 14, 1796.
10125{_}810{_}Coleridge, I love you for dedicating your poetry to Bowles.
10125{_}811{_}[1] Genius of the sacred fountain of tears, it was he who led you gently by the hand through all this valley of weeping, showed you the dark green yew-trees and the willow shades where, by the fall of waters, you might indulge in uncomplaining melancholy, a delicious regret for the past, or weave fine visions of that awful future,--    "When all the vanities of life's brief day   Oblivion's hurrying hand hath swept away,   And all its sorrows, at the awful blast   Of the archangel's trump, are but as shadows past."
10125{_}812{_}I have another sort of dedication in my head for my few things, which I want to know if you approve of and can insert.
10125{_}813{_}[2] I mean to inscribe them to my sister.
10125{_}814{_}It will be unexpected, and it will gire her pleasure; or do you think it will look whimsical at all?
10125{_}815{_}As I have not spoke to her about it, I can easily reject the idea.
10125{_}816{_}But there is a monotony in the affections which people living together, or as we do now, very frequently seeing each other, are apt to give in to,--a sort of indifference in the expression of kindness for each other, which demands that we should sometimes call to our aid the trickery of surprise.
10125{_}817{_}Do you publish with Lloyd, or without him?
10125{_}818{_}In either case my little portion may come last, and after the fashion of orders to a country correspondent, I will give directions how I should like to have 'em done.
10125{_}819{_}The title-page to stand thus:--   POEMS  BY CHARLES LAMB, OF THE INDIA HOUSE.
10125{_}820{_}Under this title the following motto, which, for want of room, I put over-leaf, and desire you to insert whether you like it or no.
10125{_}821{_}May not a gentleman choose what arms, mottoes, or armorial bearings the herald will give him leave, without consulting his republican friend, who might advise none?
10125{_}822{_}May not a publican put up the sign of the Saracen's Head, even though his undiscerning neighbor should prefer, as more genteel, the Cat and Gridiron?
10125{_}823{_}[MOTTO.]
10125{_}824{_}"This beauty, in the blossom of my youth,   When my first fire knew no adulterate incense,   Nor I no way to flatter but my fondness,   In the best language my true tongue could tell me,   And all the broken sighs my sick heart lend me,   I sued and served.
10125{_}825{_}Long did I love this lady."
10125{_}826{_}[1]                                               MASSINGER.
10125{_}827{_}THE DEDICATION.
10125{_}828{_}THE FEW FOLLOWING POEMS,   CREATURES OF THE FANCY AND THE FEELING   IN LIFE'S MORE VACANT HOURS,   PRODUCED, FOR THE MOST PART, BY   LOVE IN IDLENESS,   ARE,   WITH ALL A BROTHER'S FONDNESS,   INSCRIBED TO    MARY ANN LAMB,    THE AUTHOR'S BEST FRIEND ANB SISTER.
10125{_}829{_}This is the pomp and paraphernalia of parting, with which I take my leave of a passion which has reigned so royally (so long) within me; thus, with its trappings of laureateship, I fling it off, pleased and satisfied with myself that the weakness troubles me no longer.
10125{_}830{_}I am wedded.
10125{_}831{_}Coleridge, to the fortunes of my sister and my poor old father.
10125{_}832{_}Oh, my friend, I think sometimes, could I recall the days that are past, which among them should I choose?
10125{_}833{_}Not, those "merrier days," not the "pleasant days of hope," not "those wanderings with a fair-hair'd maid," [2] which I have so often, and so feelingly regretted, but the days, Coleridge, of a _mother's_ fondness for her _schoolboy_.
10125{_}834{_}What would I give to call her back to earth for _one_ day, on my knees to ask her pardon for all those little asperities of temper which from time to time have given her gentle spirit pain.
10125{_}835{_}And the day, my friend, I trust will come; there will be "time enough" for kind offices of love, if "Heaven's eternal year" be ours.
10125{_}836{_}Hereafter, her meek spirit shall not reproach me.
10125{_}837{_}Oh, my friend, cultivate the filial feelings, and let no man think himself released from the kind "charities" of relationship.
10125{_}838{_}These shall give him peace at the last; these are the best foundation for every species of benevolence.
10125{_}839{_}I rejoice to hear, by certain channels, that you, my friend, are reconciled with all your relations.
10125{_}840{_}'T is the most kindly and natural species of love, and we have all the associated train of early feelings to secure its strength and perpetuity.
10125{_}841{_}Send me an account of your health; _indeed_ I am solicitous about you.
10125{_}842{_}God love you and yours!
10125{_}843{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}844{_}[1] From "A Very Woman."
10125{_}845{_}[2] An allusion to Lamb's first love,--the "Anna" of his sonnets, and the original, probably, of "Rosamund Gray" and of "Alice W---n" in the beautiful essay "Dream Children."
10125{_}846{_}[3] The earliest sonnets of William Lisle Bowles were published in 1789, the year of Lamb's removal from Christ's Hospital.
10125{_}847{_}[4] Alluding to the prospective joint volume of poems (by Coleridge, Lamb, and Charles Lloyd) to be published by Cottle in 1797.
10125{_}848{_}This was Lamb's second serious literary venture, he and Coleridge having issued a joint volume in 1796.
10125{_}849{_}IX.
10125{_}850{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}851{_}[Fragment.]
10125{_}852{_}_Dec_.
10125{_}853{_}5, 1796.
10125{_}854{_}At length I have done with verse-making,--not that I relish other people's poetry less: theirs comes from 'em without effort; mine is the difficult operation of a brain scanty of ideas, made more difficult by disuse.
10125{_}855{_}I have been reading "The Task" with fresh delight.
10125{_}856{_}I am glad you love Cowper.
10125{_}857{_}I could forgive a man for not enjoying Milton; but I would not call that man my friend who should be offended with the "divine chit-chat of Cowper."
10125{_}858{_}Write to me.
10125{_}859{_}God love you and yours!
10125{_}860{_}C. L.    X.
10125{_}861{_}TO COLERIDGE,  _Dec_.
10125{_}862{_}10, 1796.
10125{_}863{_}I had put my letter into the post rather hastily, not expecting to have to acknowledge another from you so soon.
10125{_}864{_}This morning's present has made me alive again.
10125{_}865{_}My last night's epistle was childishly querulous: but you have put a little life into me, and I will thank you for your remembrance of me, while my sense of it is yet warm; for if I linger a day or two, I may use the same phrase of acknowledgment, or similar, but the feeling that dictates it now will be gone; I shall send you a _caput mortuum_; not a _cor vivens_.
10125{_}866{_}Thy "Watchman's," thy bellman's verses, I do retort upon thee, thou libellous varlet,--why, you cried the hours yourself, and who made you so proud?
10125{_}867{_}But I submit, to show my humility, most implicitly to your dogmas, I reject entirely the copy of verses you reject.
10125{_}868{_}With regard to my leaving off versifying [1] you have said so many pretty things, so many fine compliments, Ingeniously decked out in the garb of sincerity, and undoubtedly springing from a present feeling somewhat like sincerity, that you might melt the most un-muse-ical soul, did you not (now for a Rowland compliment for your profusion of Olivers),--did you not in your very epistle, by the many pretty fancies and profusion of heart displayed in it, dissuade and discourage me from attempting anything after you.
10125{_}869{_}At present I have not leisure to make verses, nor anything approaching to a fondness for the exercise.
10125{_}870{_}In the ignorant present time, who can answer for the future man?
10125{_}871{_}"At lovers' perjuries Jove laughs,"--and poets have sometimes a disingenuous way of forswearing their occupation.
10125{_}872{_}This, though, is not my case.
10125{_}873{_}The tender cast of soul, sombred with melancholy and subsiding recollections, is favorable to the Sonnet or the Elegy; but from--    "The sainted growing woof   The teasing troubles keep aloof."
10125{_}874{_}The music of poesy may charm for a while the importunate, teasing cares of life; but the teased and troubled man is not in a disposition to make that music.
10125{_}875{_}You sent me some very sweet lines relative to Burns; but it was at a time when, in my highly agitated and perhaps distorted state of mind, I thought it a duty to read 'em hastily and burn 'em.
10125{_}876{_}I burned all my own verses, all my book of extracts from Beaumont and Fletcher and a thousand sources; I burned a little journal of my foolish passion which I had a long time kept,--    "Noting, ere they past away,   The little lines of yesterday."
10125{_}877{_}I almost burned all your letters; I did as bad,--I lent 'em to a friend to keep out of my brother's sight, should he come and make inquisition into our papers; for much as he dwelt upon your conversation while you were among us, and delighted to be with you, it has been, his fashion, ever since to depreciate and cry you down,--you were the cause of my madness, you and your damned foolish sensibility and melancholy; and he lamented with a true brotherly feeling that we ever met,--even as the sober citizen, when his son went astray upon the mountains of Parnassus, is said to have cursed wit, and poetry, and Pope.
10125{_}878{_}[2] I quote wrong, but no matter.
10125{_}879{_}These letters I lent to a friend to be out of the way for a season; but I have claimed them in vain, and shall not cease to regret their loss.
10125{_}880{_}Your packets posterior to the date of my misfortunes, commencing with that valuable consolatory epistle, are every day accumulating,--they are sacred things with me.
10125{_}881{_}Publish your _Burns_ [3] when and how you like; it will "be new to me,"--my memory of it is very confused, and tainted with unpleasant associations.
10125{_}882{_}Burns was the god of my idolatry, as Bowles of yours.
10125{_}883{_}I am jealous of your fraternizing with Bowles, when I think you relish him more than Burns or my old favorite, Cowper, But you conciliate matters when you talk of the "divine chit-chat" of the latter; by the expression I see you thoroughly relish him.
10125{_}884{_}I love Mrs. Coleridge for her excuses an hundred-fold more dearly than if she heaped "line upon line," out-Hannah-ing Hannah More, and had rather hear you sing "Did a very little baby" by your family fireside, than listen to you when you were repeating one of Bowles's sweetest sonnets in your sweet manner, while we two were indulging sympathy, a solitary luxury, by the fireside at the "Salutation."
10125{_}885{_}Yet have I no higher ideas of heaven.
10125{_}886{_}Your company was one "cordial in this melancholy vale,"--the remembrance of it is a blessing partly, and partly a curse.
10125{_}887{_}When I can abstract myself from things present, I can enjoy it with a freshness of relish; but it more constantly operates to an unfavorable comparison with the uninteresting converse I always and _only_ can partake in.
10125{_}888{_}Not a soul loves Bowles here; scarce one has heard of Burns; few but laugh at me for reading my Testament,--they talk a language I understand not; I conceal sentiments that would be a puzzle to them.
10125{_}889{_}I can only converse with you by letter, and with the dead in their books.
10125{_}890{_}My sister, indeed, is all I can wish in a companion; but our spirits are alike poorly, our reading and knowledge from the selfsame sources, our communication with the scenes of the world alike narrow.
10125{_}891{_}Never having kept separate company, or any "company _together_;" never having read separate books, and few books _together_,--what knowledge have we to convey to each other?
10125{_}892{_}In our little range of duties and connections, how few sentiments can take place without friends, with few books, with a taste for religion rather than a strong religious habit!
10125{_}893{_}We need some support, some leading-strings to cheer and direct us.
10125{_}894{_}You talk very wisely; and be not sparing of _your advice_.
10125{_}895{_}Continue to remember us, and to show us you do remember us; we will take as lively an interest in what concerns you and yours.
10125{_}896{_}All I can add to your happiness will be sympathy.
10125{_}897{_}You can add to mine _more_; you can teach me wisdom.
10125{_}898{_}I am indeed an unreasonable correspondent: but I was unwilling to let my last night's letter go off without this qualifier: you will perceive by this my mind is easier, and you will rejoice.
10125{_}899{_}I do not expect or wish you to write till you are moved; and of course shall not, till you announce to me that event, think of writing myself.
10125{_}900{_}Love to Mrs. Coleridge and David Hartley, and my kind remembrance to Lloyd, if he is with you.
10125{_}901{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}902{_}[1] See preceding letter.
10125{_}903{_}[2] Epistle to Arbuthnot:--    "Poor Cornus sees his frantic wife elope,   And curses wit, and poetry, and Pope."
10125{_}904{_}[3] The lines on him which Coleridge had sent to Lamb, and which the latter had burned.
10125{_}905{_}XI.
10125{_}906{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}907{_}_January_ 5, 1797.
10125{_}908{_}_Sunday Morning_.--You cannot surely mean to degrade the Joan of Arc into a pot-girl.
10125{_}909{_}[1] You are not going, I hope, to annex to that most splendid ornament of Southey's poem all this cock-and-a-bull story of Joan, the publican's daughter of Neufchatel, with the lamentable episode of a wagoner, his wife, and six children.
10125{_}910{_}The texture will be most lamentably disproportionate.
10125{_}911{_}The first forty or fifty lines of these addenda are no doubt in their way admirable too; but many would prefer the Joan of Southey.
10125{_}912{_}[1] Coleridge, in later years, indorsed Lamb's opinion of this portion of his contribution to "Joan of Arc."
10125{_}913{_}"I was really astonished," he said, "(1) at the schoolboy, wretched, allegoric machinery; (2) at the transmogrification of the fanatic virago into a modern novel-pawing proselyte of the "Age of Reason,"--a Tom Paine in petticoats; (3) at the utter want of all rhythm in the verse, the monotony and dead plumb-down of the pauses, and the absence of all bone, muscle, and sinew in the single lines."
10125{_}914{_}"On mightiest deeds to brood   Of shadowy vastness, such as made my heart   Throb fast; anon I paused, and in a state   Of half expectance listened to the wind."
10125{_}915{_}"They wondered at me, who had known me once   A cheerful, careless damsel."
10125{_}916{_}"The eye,   That of the circling throng and of the visible world,   Unseeing, saw the shapes of holy phantasy."
10125{_}917{_}I see nothing in your description of the Maid equal to these.
10125{_}918{_}There is a fine originality certainly in those lines,--    "For she had lived in this bad world   As in a place of tombs,   And touched not the pollutions of the dead;"  but your "fierce vivacity" is a faint copy of the "fierce and terrible benevolence" of Southey; added to this, that it will look like rivalship in you, and extort a comparison with Southey,--I think to your disadvantage.
10125{_}919{_}And the lines, considered in themselves as an addition to what you had before written (strains of a far higher mood), are but such as Madame Fancy loves in some of her more familiar moods,--at such times as she has met Noll Goldsmith, and walked and talked with him, calling him "old acquaintance."
10125{_}920{_}Southey certainly has no pretensions to vie with you in the sublime of poetry; but he tells a plain tale better than you.
10125{_}921{_}I will enumerate some woful blemishes, some of 'em sad deviations from that simplicity which was your aim.
10125{_}922{_}"Hailed who might be near" (the "canvas-coverture moving," by the by, is laughable); "a woman and six children" (by the way, why not nine children?
10125{_}923{_}It would have been just half as pathetic again); "statues of sleep they seemed;" "frost-mangled wretch;" "green putridity;" "hailed him immortal" (rather ludicrous again); "voiced a sad and simple tale" (abominable!
10125{_}924{_}); "unprovendered;" "such his tale;" "Ah, suffering to the height of what was sufffered" (a most _insufferable line_); "amazements of affright;" "The hot, sore brain attributes its own hues of ghastliness and torture" (what shocking confusion of ideas!).
10125{_}925{_}In these delineations of common and natural feelings, in the familiar walks of poetry, you seem to resemble Montauban dancing with Roubigne's tenants [1], "_much of his native loftiness remained in the execution_."
10125{_}926{_}I was reading your "Religious Musings" the other day, and sincerely I think it the noblest poem in the language next after the "Paradise Lost;" and even that was not made the vehicle of such grand truths.
10125{_}927{_}"There is one mind," etc., down to "Almighty's throne," are without a rival in the whole compass of my poetical reading.
10125{_}928{_}"Stands in the sun, and with no partial gaze   Views all creation."
10125{_}929{_}I wish I could have written those lines.
10125{_}930{_}I rejoice that I am able to relish them.
10125{_}931{_}The loftier walks of Pindus are your proper region.
10125{_}932{_}There you have no compeer in modern times.
10125{_}933{_}Leave the lowlands, unenvied, in possession of such men as Cowper and Southey.
10125{_}934{_}Thus am I pouring balsam into the wounds I may have been inflicting on my poor friend's vanity.
10125{_}935{_}In your notice of Southey's new volume you omit to mention the most pleasing of all, the "Miniature."
10125{_}936{_}"There were   Who formed high hopes and flattering ones of thee,   Young Robert!"
10125{_}937{_}"Spirit of Spenser!
10125{_}938{_}was the wanderer wrong?"
10125{_}939{_}Fairfax I have been in quest of a long time.
10125{_}940{_}Johnson, in his "Life of Waller," gives a most delicious specimen of him, and adds, in the true manner of that delicate critic, as well as amiable man, "It may be presumed that this old version will not be much read after the elegant translation of my friend Mr.
10125{_}941{_}Hoole."
10125{_}942{_}I endeavored--I wished to gain some idea of Tasso from this Mr. Hoole, the great boast and ornament of the India House, but soon desisted.
10125{_}943{_}I found him more vapid than smallest small beer "sun-vinegared."
10125{_}944{_}Your "Dream," down to that exquisite line,--    "I can't tell half his adventures,"  is a most happy resemblance of Chaucer.
10125{_}945{_}The remainder is so-so.
10125{_}946{_}The best line, I think, is, "He belong'd, I believe, to the witch Melancholy."
10125{_}947{_}By the way, when will our volume come out?
10125{_}948{_}Don't delay it till you have written a new "Joan of Arc."
10125{_}949{_}Send what letters you please by me, and in any way you choose, single or double.
10125{_}950{_}The India Company is better adapted to answer the cost than the generality of my friend's correspondents,--such poor and honest dogs as John Thelwall particularly.
10125{_}951{_}I cannot say I know Coulson,--at least intimately; I once supped with him and Austin; I think his manners very pleasing.
10125{_}952{_}I will not tell you what I think of Lloyd, for he may by chance come to see this letter; and that thought puts a restraint on me.
10125{_}953{_}I cannot think what subject would suit your epic genius,--some philosophical subject, I conjecture, in which shall be blended the sublime of poetry and of science.
10125{_}954{_}Your proposed "Hymns" will be a fit preparatory study wherewith "to discipline your young novitiate soul."
10125{_}955{_}I grow dull; I'll go walk myself out of my dulness.
10125{_}956{_}_Sunday Night_,--You and Sara are very good to think so kindly and so favorably of poor Mary; I would to God all did so too.
10125{_}957{_}But I very much fear she must not think of coming home in my father's lifetime.
10125{_}958{_}It is very hard upon her, but our circumstances are peculiar, and we must submit to them, God be praised she is so well as she is.
10125{_}959{_}She bears her situation as one who has no right to complain.
10125{_}960{_}My poor old aunt, whom you have seen, the kindest, goodest creature to me when I was at school; who used to toddle there to bring me good things, when I, schoolboy-like, only despised her for it, and used to be ashamed to see her come and sit herself down on the old coal-hole steps as you went into the old grammar-school, and open her apron, and bring out her basin, with some nice thing she had caused to be saved for me, [2]--the good old creature is now lying on her death-bed.
10125{_}961{_}I cannot bear to think on her deplorable state.
10125{_}962{_}To the shock she received on that our evil day, from which she never completely recovered, I impute her illness.
10125{_}963{_}She says, poor thing, she is glad she is come home to die with me.
10125{_}964{_}I was always her favourite;    "No after friendship e'er can raise   The endearments of our early days;   Nor e'er the heart such fondness prove,   As when it first began to love."
10125{_}965{_}[1] In Mackenzie's tale, "Julia de Roubigne."
10125{_}966{_}[2] See the essay, "Christ's Hospital Five-and-Thirty Years Ago."
10125{_}967{_}XII.
10125{_}968{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}969{_}_January_ 10, 1797.
10125{_}970{_}I need not repeat my wishes to have my little sonnets printed _verbatim_ my last way.
10125{_}971{_}In particular, I fear lest you should prefer printing my first sonnet, as you have done more than once, "did the wand of Merlin wave," it looks so like Mr. Merlin, [1] the ingenious successor of the immortal Merlin, now living in good health and spirits, and flourishing in magical reputation, in Oxford Street; and, on my life, one half who read it would understand it so.
10125{_}972{_}Do put 'em forth finally, as I have, in various letters, settled it; for first a man's self is to be pleased, and then his friends,--and of course the greater number of his friends, if they differ _inter se_.
10125{_}973{_}Thus taste may safely be put to the vote.
10125{_}974{_}I do long to see our names together,--not for vanity's sake, and naughty pride of heart altogether; for not a living soul I know, or am intimate with, will scarce read the book,--so I shall gain nothing, _quoad famam_; and yet there is a little vanity mixes in it, I cannot help denying.--I am aware of the unpoetical cast of the last six lines of my last sonnet, and think myself unwarranted in smuggling so tame a thing into the book; only the sentiments of those six lines are thoroughly congenial to me in my state of mind, and I wish to accumulate perpetuating tokens of my affection to poor Mary.
10125{_}975{_}That it has no originality in its cast, nor anything in the feelings but what is common and natural to thousands, nor ought properly to be called poetry, I see; still, it will tend to keep present to my mind a view of things which I ought to indulge.
10125{_}976{_}These six lines, too, have not, to a reader, a connectedness with the foregoing.
10125{_}977{_}Omit it if you like,--What a treasure it is to my poor, indolent, and unemployed mind thus to lay hold on a subject to talk about, though 'tis but a sonnet, and that of the lowest order!
10125{_}978{_}How mournfully inactive I am!--'Tis night; good night.
10125{_}979{_}My sister, I thank God, is nigh recovered; she was seriously ill. Do, in your next letter, and that right soon, give me some satisfaction respecting your present situation at Stowey.
10125{_}980{_}Is it a farm that you have got?
10125{_}981{_}and what does your worship know about farming?
10125{_}982{_}Coleridge, I want you to write an epic poem.
10125{_}983{_}Nothing short of it can satisfy the vast capacity of true poetic genius.
10125{_}984{_}Having one great end to direct all your poetical faculties to, and on which to lay out your hopes, your ambition will show you to what you are equal.
10125{_}985{_}By the sacred energies of Milton!
10125{_}986{_}by the dainty, sweet, and soothing phantasies of honey-tongued Spenser!
10125{_}987{_}I adjure you to attempt the epic, or do something more ample than the writing an occasional brief ode or sonnet; something "to make yourself forever known,--to make the age to come your own."
10125{_}988{_}But I prate; doubtless you meditate something.
10125{_}989{_}When you are exalted among the lords of epic fame, I shall recall with pleasure and exultingly the days of your humility, when you disdained not to put forth, in the same volume with mine, your "Religious Musings" and that other poem from the "Joan of Arc," those promising first-fruits of high renown to come.
10125{_}990{_}You have learning, you have fancy, you have enthusiasm, you have strength and amplitude of wing enow for flights like those I recommend.
10125{_}991{_}In the vast and unexplored regions of fairy-land there is ground enough unfound and uncultivated: search there, and realize your favorite Susquehanna scheme.
10125{_}992{_}In all our comparisons of taste, I do not know whether I have ever heard your opinion of a poet very dear to me,--the now-out-of-fashion Cowley.
10125{_}993{_}Favor me with your judgment of him, and tell me if his prose essays, in particular, as well as no inconsiderable part of his verse, be not delicious.
10125{_}994{_}I prefer the graceful rambling of his essays even to the courtly elegance and ease of Addison, abstracting from this the latter's exquisite humor.
10125{_}995{_}When the little volume is printed, send me three or four, at all events not more than six, copies, and tell me if I put you to any additional expense by printing with you, I have no thought of the kind, and in that case must reimburse you.
10125{_}996{_}Priestley, whom I sin in almost adoring, speaks of "such a choice of company as tends to keep up that, right bent and firmness of mind which a necessary intercourse with the world would otherwise warp and relax....
10125{_}997{_}Such fellowship is the true balsam of life; its cement is infinitely more durable than that of the friendships of the world, and it looks for its proper fruit and complete gratification to the life beyond the grave."
10125{_}998{_}Is there a possible chance for such an one as I to realize in this world such friendships?
10125{_}999{_}Where am I to look for 'em?
10125{_}1000{_}What testimonials shall I bring of my being worthy of such friendship?
10125{_}1001{_}Alas!
10125{_}1002{_}the great and good go together in separate herds, and leave such as I to lag far, far behind in all intellectual and, far more grievous to say, in all moral accomplishments.
10125{_}1003{_}Coleridge, I have not one truly elevated character among my acquaintance,--not one Christian; not one but undervalues Christianity.
10125{_}1004{_}Singly what am I to do?
10125{_}1005{_}Wesley (have you read his life?
10125{_}1006{_}), was _he_ not an elevated character?
10125{_}1007{_}Wesley has said, "Religion is not a solitary thing."
10125{_}1008{_}Alas!
10125{_}1009{_}it necessarily is so with me, or next to solitary.
10125{_}1010{_}'T is true you write to me.
10125{_}1011{_}But correspondence by letter and personal intimacy are very widely different.
10125{_}1012{_}Do, do write to me, and do some good to my mind, already how much "warped and relaxed" by the world!
10125{_}1013{_}'T is the conclusion of another evening.
10125{_}1014{_}Good night; God have us all in His keeping!
10125{_}1015{_}If you are sufficiently at leisure, oblige me with an account of your plan of life at Stowey; your literary occupations and prospects,--in short, make me acquainted with every circumstance which, as relating to you, can be interesting to me.
10125{_}1016{_}Are you yet a Berkleyan?
10125{_}1017{_}Make me one.
10125{_}1018{_}I rejoice in being, speculatively, a necessarian.
10125{_}1019{_}Would to God I were habitually a practical one!
10125{_}1020{_}Confirm me in the faith of that great and glorious doctrine, and keep me steady in the contemplation of it.
10125{_}1021{_}You some time since expressed an intention you had of finishing some extensive work on the Evidences of Natural and Revealed Religion.
10125{_}1022{_}Have you let that intention go?
10125{_}1023{_}Or are you doing anything towards it?
10125{_}1024{_}Make to yourself other ten talents.
10125{_}1025{_}My letter is full of nothingness.
10125{_}1026{_}I talk of nothing.
10125{_}1027{_}But I must talk.
10125{_}1028{_}I love to write to you.
10125{_}1029{_}I take a pride in it.
10125{_}1030{_}It makes me think less meanly of myself.
10125{_}1031{_}It makes me think myself not totally disconnected from the better part of mankind.
10125{_}1032{_}I know I am too dissatisfied with the beings around me; but I cannot help occasionally exclaiming, "Woe is me, that I am constrained to dwell with Meshech, and to have my habitation among the tents of Kedar."
10125{_}1033{_}I know I am noways better in practice than my neighors, but I have a taste for religion, an occasional earnest aspiration after perfection, which they have not.
10125{_}1034{_}I gain, nothing by being with such as myself,--we encourage one another in mediocrity, I am always longing to be with men more excellent than myself.
10125{_}1035{_}All this must sound odd to you; but these are my predominant feelings when I sit down to write to you, and I should put force upon my mind, were I to reject them, Yet I rejoice, and feel my privilege with gratitude, when I have been reading some wise book, such as I have just been reading,--Priestley on Philosophical Necessity,--in the thought that I enjoy a kind of communion, a kind of friendship even, with the great and good.
10125{_}1036{_}Books are to me instead of friends, I wish they did not resemble the latter in their scarceness.
10125{_}1037{_}And how does little David Hartley?
10125{_}1038{_}"Ecquid in antiquam virtutem?"
10125{_}1039{_}Does his mighty name work wonders yet upon his little frame and opening mind?
10125{_}1040{_}I did not distinctly understand you,--you don't mean to make an actual ploughman of him?
10125{_}1041{_}Is Lloyd with you yet?
10125{_}1042{_}Are you intimate with Southey?
10125{_}1043{_}What poems is he about to publish?
10125{_}1044{_}He hath a most prolific brain, and is indeed a most sweet poet.
10125{_}1045{_}But how can you answer all the various mass of interrogation I have put to you in the course of the sheet?
10125{_}1046{_}Write back just what you like, only write something, however brief.
10125{_}1047{_}I have now nigh finished my page, and got to the end of another evening (Monday evening), and my eyes are heavy and sleepy, and my brain unsuggestive.
10125{_}1048{_}I have just heart enough awake to say good night once more, and God love you, my dear friend; God love us all!
10125{_}1049{_}Mary bears an affectionate remembrance of you.
10125{_}1050{_}CHARLES LAMB.
10125{_}1051{_}[1] A well-known conjuror of the time.
10125{_}1052{_}XIII.
10125{_}1053{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}1054{_}_February_ 13, 1797.
10125{_}1055{_}Your poem is altogether admirable--parts of it are even exquisite; in particular your personal account of the Maid far surpasses anything of the sort in Southey.
10125{_}1056{_}[1] I perceived all its excellences, on a first reading, as readily as now you have been removing a supposed film from my eyes.
10125{_}1057{_}I was only struck with a certain faulty disproportion in the matter and the _style_, which I still think I perceive, between these lines and the former ones.
10125{_}1058{_}I had an end in view,--I wished to make you reject the poem, only as being discordant with the other; and, in subservience to that end, it was politically done in me to over-pass, and make no mention of, merit which, could you think me capable of _overlooking_, might reasonably damn forever in your judgment all pretensions in me to be critical.
10125{_}1059{_}There, I will be judged by Lloyd whether I have not made a very handsome recantation.
10125{_}1060{_}I was in the case of a man whose friend has asked him his opinion of a certain young lady; the deluded wight gives judgment against her _in toto_,--don't like her face, her walk, her manners; finds fault with her eyebrows; can see no wit in her.
10125{_}1061{_}His friend looks blank; he begins to smell a rat; wind veers about; he acknowledges her good sense, her judgment in dress, a certain simplicity of manners and honesty of heart, something too in her manners which gains upon you after a short acquaintance;--and then her accurate pronunciation of the French language, and a pretty, uncultivated taste in drawing.
10125{_}1062{_}The reconciled gentleman smiles applause, squeezes him by the hand, and hopes he will do him the honor of taking a bit of dinner with Mrs. ---- and him--a plain family dinner--some day next week; "for, I suppose, you never heard we were married.
10125{_}1063{_}I'm glad to see you like my wife, however; you 'll come and see her, ha?"
10125{_}1064{_}Now am I too proud to retract entirely?
10125{_}1065{_}Yet I do perceive I am in some sort straitened; you are manifestly wedded, to this poem, and what fancy has joined, let no man separate, I turn me to the "Joan of Arc," second book.
10125{_}1066{_}The solemn openings of it are with sounds which, Lloyd would say, "are silence to the mind."
10125{_}1067{_}The deep preluding strains are fitted to initiate the mind, with a pleasing awe, into the sublimest mysteries of theory concerning man's nature and his noblest destination,--the philosophy of a first cause; of subordinate agents in creation superior to man; the subserviency of pagan worship and pagan faith to the introduction of a purer and more perfect religion, which you so elegantly describe as winning, with gradual steps, her difficult way northward from Bethabara.
10125{_}1068{_}After all this cometh Joan, a _publican's_ daughter, sitting on an ale-house _bench_, and marking the _swingings_ of the _signboard_, finding a poor man, his wife and six children, starved to death with cold, and thence roused into a state of mind proper to receive visions emblematical of equality,--which, what the devil Joan had to do with, I don't know, or indeed with the French and American revolutions; though that needs no pardon, it is executed so nobly.
10125{_}1069{_}After all, if you perceive no disproportion, all argument is vain; I do not so much object to parts.
10125{_}1070{_}Again, when you talk of building your fame on these lines in preference to the "Religious Musings," I cannot help conceiving of you and of the author of that as two different persons, and I think you a very vain man.
10125{_}1071{_}I have been re-reading your letter.
10125{_}1072{_}Much of it I _could_ dispute; but with the latter part of it, in which you compare the two Joans with respect to their predispositions for fanaticism, I _toto corde_ coincide; only I think that Southey's strength rather lies in the description of the emotions of the Maid under the weight of inspiration.
10125{_}1073{_}These (I see no mighty difference between _her_ describing them or _you_ describing them),--these if you only equal, the previous admirers of his poem, as is natural, will prefer his; if you surpass, prejudice will scarcely allow it, and I scarce think you will surpass, though your specimen at the conclusion (I am in earnest) I think very nigh equals them.
10125{_}1074{_}And in an account of a fanatic or of a prophet the description of her _emotions_ is expected to be most highly finished.
10125{_}1075{_}By the way, I spoke far too disparagingly of your lines, and, I am ashamed to say.
10125{_}1076{_}purposely, I should like you to specify or particularize; the story of the "Tottering Eld," of "his eventful years all come and gone," is too general; why not make him a soldier, or some character, however, in which he has been witness to frequency of "cruel wrong and strange distress"?
10125{_}1077{_}I think I should, When I laughed at the "miserable man crawling from beneath the coverture," I wonder I did not perceive it was a laugh of horror,--such as I have laughed at Dante's picture of the famished Ugolino.
10125{_}1078{_}Without falsehood, I perceive an hundred, beauties in your narrative.
10125{_}1079{_}Yet I wonder you do not perceive something out-of-the-way, something unsimple and artificial, in the expression, "voiced a sad tale."
10125{_}1080{_}I hate made-dishes at the muses' banquet, I believe I was wrong in most of my other objections.
10125{_}1081{_}But surely "hailed him immortal" adds nothing to the terror of the man's death, which it was your business to heighten, not diminish by a phrase which takes away all terror from it, I like that line, "They closed their eyes in sleep, nor knew 'twas death," Indeed, there is scarce a line I do not like, "_Turbid_ ecstasy" is surely not so good as what you had written,--"troublous."
10125{_}1082{_}"Turbid" rather suits the muddy kind of inspiration which London porter confers.
10125{_}1083{_}The versification is throughout, to my ears, unexceptionable, with no disparagement to the measure of the "Religious Musings," which is exactly fitted to the thoughts.
10125{_}1084{_}You were building your house on a rock when you rested your fame on that poem.
10125{_}1085{_}I can scarce bring myself to believe that I am admitted to a familiar correspondence, and all the license of friendship, with a man who writes blank verse like Milton.
10125{_}1086{_}Now, this is delicate flattery, _indirect_ flattery.
10125{_}1087{_}Go on with your "Maid of Orleans," and be content to be second to yourself.
10125{_}1088{_}I shall become a convert to it, when 'tis finished.
10125{_}1089{_}This afternoon I attend the funeral of my poor old aunt, who died on Thursday.
10125{_}1090{_}I own I am thankful that the good creature has ended all her days of suffering and infirmity.
10125{_}1091{_}She was to me the "cherisher of infancy;" and one must fall on these occasions into reflections, which it would be commonplace to enumerate, concerning death, "of chance and change, and fate in human life."
10125{_}1092{_}Good God, who could have foreseen all this but four months back!
10125{_}1093{_}I had reckoned, in particular, on my aunt's living many years; she was a very hearty old woman.
10125{_}1094{_}But she was a mere skeleton before she died; looked more like a corpse that had lain weeks in the grave, than one fresh dead.
10125{_}1095{_}"Truly the light is sweet, and a pleasant thing it is for the eyes to behold the sun: but let a man live many days, and rejoice in them all; yet let him remember the days of darkness, for they shall be many."
10125{_}1096{_}Coleridge, why are we to live on after all the strength and beauty of existence are gone, when all the life of life is fled, as poor Burns expresses it?
10125{_}1097{_}Tell Lloyd I have had thoughts of turning Quaker, and have been reading, or am rather just beginning to read, a most capital book, good thoughts in good language, William Penn's "No Cross, no Crown;" I like it immensely.
10125{_}1098{_}Unluckily I went to one of his meetings, tell hire, in St. John Street, yesterday, and saw a man under all the agitations and workings of a fanatic, who believed himself under the influence of some "inevitable presence."
10125{_}1099{_}This cured me of Quakerism: I love it in the books of Penn and Woolman, but I detest the vanity of a man thinking he speaks by the Spirit, when what he says an ordinary man might say without all that quaking and trembling.
10125{_}1100{_}In the midst of his inspiration,--and the effects of it were most noisy,--was handed into the midst of the meeting a most terrible blackguard Wapping sailor; the poor man, I believe, had rather have been in the hottest part of an engagement, for the congregation of broad-brims, together with the ravings of the prophet, were too much for his gravity, though I saw even he had delicacy enough not to laugh out.
10125{_}1101{_}And the inspired gentleman, though his manner was so supernatural, yet neither talked, nor professed to talk anything more than good sober sense, common morality, with, now and then a declaration of not speaking from himself.
10125{_}1102{_}Among other things, looking back to this childhood and early youth, he told the meeting what a graceless young dog he had been, that in his youth he had a good share of wit.
10125{_}1103{_}Reader, if thou hadst seen the gentleman, thou wouldst have sworn that it must indeed have been many years ago, for his rueful physiognomy would have scared away the playful goddess from the meeting, where he presided, forever, A wit!
10125{_}1104{_}a wit!
10125{_}1105{_}what could he mean?
10125{_}1106{_}Lloyd, it minded me of Falkland in the "Rivals," "Am I full of wit and humor?
10125{_}1107{_}No, indeed, you are not.
10125{_}1108{_}Am I the life and soul of every company I come into?
10125{_}1109{_}No, it cannot be said you are."
10125{_}1110{_}That hard-faced gentleman a wit!
10125{_}1111{_}Why, Nature wrote on his fanatic forehead fifty years ago, "Wit never comes, that comes to all."
10125{_}1112{_}I should be as scandalized at a _bon-mot_ issuing from his oracle-looking mouth as to see Cato go down a country-dance.
10125{_}1113{_}God love you all!
10125{_}1114{_}You are very good to submit to be pleased with reading my nothings.
10125{_}1115{_}'T is the privilege of friendship to talk nonsense and to have her nonsense respected.
10125{_}1116{_}Yours ever,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}1117{_}[1] See Letter VIII.
10125{_}1118{_}XIV.
10125{_}1119{_}TO COLERIDGE.
10125{_}1120{_}_January_ 28, 1798.
10125{_}1121{_}You have writ me many kind letters, and I have answered none of them.
10125{_}1122{_}I don't deserve your attentions.
10125{_}1123{_}An unnatural indifference has been creeping on me since my last misfortunes, or I should have seized the first opening of a correspondence with _you_.
10125{_}1124{_}To you I owe much under God.
10125{_}1125{_}In my brief acquaintance with you in London, your conversations won me to the better cause, and rescued me from the polluting spirit of the world.
10125{_}1126{_}I might have been a worthless character without you; as it is, I do possess a certain improvable portion of devotional feelings, though when I view myself in the light of divine truth, and not according to the common measures of human judgment.
10125{_}1127{_}I am altogether corrupt and sinful.
10125{_}1128{_}This is no cant.
10125{_}1129{_}I am very sincere.
10125{_}1130{_}These last afflictions, [1] Coleridge, have failed to soften and bend my will.
10125{_}1131{_}They found me unprepared.
10125{_}1132{_}My former calamities produced in me a spirit of humility and a spirit of prayer.
10125{_}1133{_}I thought they had sufficiently disciplined me; but the event ought to humble me.
10125{_}1134{_}If God's judgments now fail to take away from the the heart of stone, what more grievous trials ought I not to expect?
10125{_}1135{_}I have been very querulous, impatient under the rod, full of little jealousies and heartburnings.
10125{_}1136{_}I had wellnigh quarrelled with Charles Lloyd, and for no other reason, I believe, than that the good creature did all he could to make me happy.
10125{_}1137{_}The truth is, I thought he tried to force my mind from its natural and proper bent: he continually wished me to be from home; he was drawing me _from_ the consideration of my poor dear Mary's situation, rather than assisting me to gain a proper view of it with religious consolations.
10125{_}1138{_}I wanted to be left to the tendency of my own mind in a solitary state which, in times past, I knew had led to quietness and a patient bearing of the yoke.
10125{_}1139{_}He was hurt that I was not more constantly with him; but he was living with White,--a man to whom I had never been accustomed to impart my _dearest feelings_; though from long habits of friendliness, and many a social and good quality, I loved him very much, I met company there sometimes,--indiscriminate company.
10125{_}1140{_}Any society almost, when I am in affliction, is sorely painful to me.
10125{_}1141{_}I seem to breathe more freely, to think more collectedly, to feel more properly and calmly, when alone.
10125{_}1142{_}All these things the good creature did with the kindest intentions in the world, but they produced in me nothing but soreness and discontent.
10125{_}1143{_}I became, as he complained, "jaundiced" towards him....
10125{_}1144{_}But he has forgiven me; and his smile, I hope, will draw all such humors from me.
10125{_}1145{_}I am recovering, God be praised for it, a healthiness of mind, something like calmness; but I want more religion, I am jealous of human helps and leaning-places.
10125{_}1146{_}I rejoice in your good fortunes.
10125{_}1147{_}May God at the last settle you!
10125{_}1148{_}You have had many and painful trials; humanly speaking, they are going to end; but we should rather pray that discipline may attend us through the whole of our lives.... A careless and a dissolute spirit has advanced upon _me_ with large strides.
10125{_}1149{_}Pray God that my present afflictions may be sanctified to me!
10125{_}1150{_}Mary is recovering; but I see no opening yet of a situation for her.
10125{_}1151{_}Your invitation went to my very heart; but you have a power of exciting interest, of leading all hearts captive, too forcible to admit of Mary's being with you.
10125{_}1152{_}I consider her as perpetually on the brink of madness.
10125{_}1153{_}I think you would almost make her dance within an inch of the precipice; she must be with duller fancies and cooler intellects.
10125{_}1154{_}I know a young man of this description who has suited her these twenty years, and may live to do so still, if we are one day restored to each other.
10125{_}1155{_}In answer to your suggestions of occupation for me, I must say that I do not think my capacity altogether suited for disquisitions of that kind....
10125{_}1156{_}I have read little; I have a very weak memory, and retain little of what I read; am unused to composition in which any methodizing is required.
10125{_}1157{_}But I thank you sincerely for the hint, and shall receive it as far as I am able,--that is, endeavor to engage my mind in some constant and innocent pursuit.
10125{_}1158{_}I know my capacities better than you do.
10125{_}1159{_}Accept my kindest love, and believe me yours, as ever.
10125{_}1160{_}C. L.  [1] Mary Lamb had fallen ill again.
10125{_}1161{_}XV.
10125{_}1162{_}TO ROBERT SOUTHEY  (No month, 1798.)
10125{_}1163{_}Dear Southey,--I thank you heartily for the eclogue [1]; it pleases me mightily, being so full of picture-work and circumstances.
10125{_}1164{_}I find no fault in it, unless perhaps that Joanna's ruin is a catastrophe too trite; and this is not the first or second time you have clothed your indignation, in verse, in a tale of ruined innocence.
10125{_}1165{_}The old lady, spinning in the sun, I hope would not disdain to claim some kindred with old Margaret.
10125{_}1166{_}I could almost wish you to vary some circumstances in the conclusion.
10125{_}1167{_}A gentleman seducer has so often been described in prose and verse: what if you had accomplished Joanna's ruin by the clumsy arts and rustic gifts of some country fellow?
10125{_}1168{_}I am thinking, I believe, of the song,--    "An old woman clothed in gray,     Whose daughter was charming and young,   And she was deluded away     By Roger's false, flattering tongue."
10125{_}1169{_}A Roger-Lothario would be a novel character; I think you might paint him very well.
10125{_}1170{_}You may think this a very silly suggestion, and so indeed it is; but, in good truth, nothing else but the first words of that foolish ballad put me upon scribbling my "Rosamund."
10125{_}1171{_}[2] But I thank you heartily for the poem.
10125{_}1172{_}Not having anything of my own to send you in return,--though, to tell truth, I am at work upon something which, if I were to cut away and garble, perhaps I might send you an extract or two that might not displease you; but I will not do that; and whether it will come to anything, I know not, for I am as slow as a Fleming painter when I compose anything.
10125{_}1173{_}I will crave leave to put down a few lines of old Christopher Marlowe's; I take them from his tragedy, "The Jew of Malta."
10125{_}1174{_}The Jew is a famous character, quite out of nature; but when we consider the terrible idea our simple ancestors had of a Jew, not more to be discommended for a certain discoloring (I think Addison calls it) than the witches and fairies of Marlowe's mighty successor.
10125{_}1175{_}The scene is betwixt Barabas, the Jew, and Ithamore, a Turkish captive exposed to sale for a slave.
10125{_}1176{_}BARABAS.
10125{_}1177{_}(_A precious rascal_.)
10125{_}1178{_}"As for myself, I walk abroad o' nights,   And kill sick people groaning under walls;   Sometimes I go about and poison wells;   And now and then, to cherish Christian thieves,   I am content to lose some of my crowns,   That I may, walking in my gallery,   See 'm go pinioned along by my door.
10125{_}1179{_}Being young, I studied physic, and began   To practise first upon the Italian;   There I enriched the priests with burials,   And always kept the sexton's arms in ure [3]   With digging graves and ringing dead men's knells.
10125{_}1180{_}And after that, was I an engineer,   And in the wars 'twixt France and Germany,   Under pretence of serving Charles the Fifth,   Slew friend and enemy with my stratagems.
10125{_}1181{_}Then after that was I an usurer,   And with extorting, cozening, forfeiting,   And tricks belonging unto brokery,   I fill'd the jails with bankrupts in a year,   And with young orphans planted hospitals,   And every moon made some or other mad;   And now and then one hang'd himself for grief,   Pinning upon his breast a long great scroll,   How I with interest tormented him."
10125{_}1182{_}Now hear Ithamore, the other gentle nature, explain how he has spent his time:--  ITHAMORE  (_A Comical Dog_.)
10125{_}1183{_}"Faith, master, in setting Christian villages on fire,   Chaining of eunuchs, binding galley-slaves.
10125{_}1184{_}One time I was an hostler in an inn,   And in the night-time secret would I steal   To travellers' chambers, and there cut their throats.
10125{_}1185{_}Once at Jerusalem, where the pilgrims kneel'd,   I strewed powder on the marble stones,   And therewithal their knees would rankle so,   That I have laugh'd a-good to see the cripples   Go limping home to Christendom on stilts."
10125{_}1186{_}BARABAS.
10125{_}1187{_}"Why, this is something."
10125{_}1188{_}There is a mixture of the ludicrous and the terrible in these lines, brimful of genius and antique invention, that at first reminded me of your old description of cruelty in hell, which was in the true Hogarthian style.
10125{_}1189{_}I need not tell _you_ that Marlowe was author of that pretty madrigal, "Come live with me, and be my Love," and of the tragedy of "Edward II.," in which are certain _lines_ unequalled in our English tongue.
10125{_}1190{_}Honest Walton mentions the said madrigal under the denomination of "certain smooth verses made long since by Kit Marlowe."
10125{_}1191{_}I am glad you have put me on the scent after old Quarles.
10125{_}1192{_}If I do not put up those eclogues, and that shortly, say I am no true-nosed hound.
10125{_}1193{_}I have had a letter from Lloyd; the young metaphysician of Caius is well, and is busy recanting the new heresy, metaphysics, for the old dogma Greek.
10125{_}1194{_}My sister, I thank you, is quite well.
10125{_}1195{_}She had a slight attack the other day, which frightened me a good deal; but it went off unaccountably.
10125{_}1196{_}Love and respects to Edith.
10125{_}1197{_}Yours sincerely,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}1198{_}[1] The eclogue was entitled "The Ruined Cottage."
10125{_}1199{_}[2] His romance.
10125{_}1200{_}"Rosamund Gray."
10125{_}1201{_}[3] Use.
10125{_}1202{_}XVI.
10125{_}1203{_}TO SOUTHEY.
10125{_}1204{_}_November_ 8, 1798.
10125{_}1205{_}I perfectly accord with your opinion of old Wither.
10125{_}1206{_}Quarles is a wittier writer, but Wither lays more hold of the heart.
10125{_}1207{_}Quarles thinks of his audience when he lectures; Wither soliloquizes in company with a full heart.
10125{_}1208{_}What wretched stuff are the "Divine Fancies" of Quarles!
10125{_}1209{_}Religion appears to him no longer valuable than it furnishes matter for quibbles and riddles; he turns God's grace into wantonness.
10125{_}1210{_}Wither is like an old friend, whose warm-heartedness and estimable qualities make us wish he possessed more genius, but at the same time make us willing to dispense with that want.
10125{_}1211{_}I always love W., and sometimes admire Q.
10125{_}1212{_}Still, that portrait is a fine one; and the extract from "The Shepherds' Hunting" places him in a starry height far above Quarles, If you wrote that review in "Crit.
10125{_}1213{_}Rev.," I am sorry you are so sparing of praise to the "Ancient Marinere;" [1] so far from calling it, as you do, with some wit but more severity, "A Dutch Attempt," etc., I call it a right English attempt, and a successful one, to dethrone German sublimity.
10125{_}1214{_}You have selected a passage fertile in unmeaning miracles, but have passed by fifty passages as miraculous as the miracles they celebrate.
10125{_}1215{_}I never so deeply felt the pathetic as in that part,--    "A spring of love gush'd from my heart,   And I bless'd them unaware."
10125{_}1216{_}It stung me into high pleasure through sufferings.
10125{_}1217{_}Lloyd does not like it; his head is too metaphysical, and your taste too correct,--at least I must allege something against you both, to excuse my own dotage,--  But you allow some elaborate beauties; you should have extracted 'em.
10125{_}1218{_}"The Ancient Marinere" plays more tricks with the mind than that last poem, which is yet one of the finest written.
10125{_}1219{_}But I am getting too dogmatical; and before I degenerate into abuse, I will conclude with assuring you that I am,  Sincerely yours,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}1220{_}[1] The "Lyrical Ballads" of Wordsworth and Coleridge had just appeared.
10125{_}1221{_}The volume contained four pieces, including the "Ancient Mariner," by Coleridge.
10125{_}1222{_}XVII.
10125{_}1223{_}TO SOUTHEY.
10125{_}1224{_}_November_ 28, 1798.
10125{_}1225{_}*       *       *       *       *   I showed my "Witch" and "Dying Lover" to   Dyer [1] last night; but George could not comprehend   how that could be poetry which did not go   upon ten feet, as George and his predecessors had   taught it to do; so George read me some lectures   on the distinguishing qualities of the Ode, the Epigram,   and the Epic, and went home to illustrate his   doctrine by correcting a proof-sheet of his own   Lyrics, George writes odes where the rhymes, like   fashionable man and wife, keep a comfortable distance   of six or eight lines apart, and calls that "observing   the laws of verse," George tells you, before   he recites, that you must listen with great attention,   or you 'll miss the rhymes.
10125{_}1226{_}I did so, and found   them pretty exact, George, speaking of the dead   Ossian, exclaimeth, "Dark are the poet's eyes," I   humbly represented to him that his own eyes were   dark, and many a living bard's besides, and recommended   "Clos'd are the poet's eyes."
10125{_}1227{_}But that   would not do, I found there was an antithesis between   the darkness of his eyes and the splendor of   his genius, and I acquiesced.
10125{_}1228{_}Your recipe for a Turk's poison is invaluable and truly Marlowish.... Lloyd objects to "shutting up the womb of his purse" in my Curse (which for a Christian witch in a Christian country is not too mild, I hope): do you object?
10125{_}1229{_}I think there is a strangeness in the idea, as well as "shaking the poor like snakes from his door," which suits the speaker.
10125{_}1230{_}Witches illustrate, as fine ladies do, from their own familiar objects, and snakes and shutting up of wombs are in their way.
10125{_}1231{_}I don't know that this last charge has been before brought against 'em, nor either the sour milk or the mandrake babe; but I affirm these be things a witch would do if she could.
10125{_}1232{_}My tragedy [2] will be a medley (as I intend it to be a medley) of laughter and tears, prose and verse, and in some places rhyme, songs, wit, pathos, humor, and if possible, sublimity,--at least, it is not a fault in my intention if it does not comprehend most of these discordant colors.
10125{_}1233{_}Heaven send they dance not the "Dance of Death!"
10125{_}1234{_}I hear that the Two Noble Englishmen [3] have parted no sooner than they set foot on German earth; but I have not heard the reason,--possibly to give novelists a handle to exclaim, "Ah me, what things are perfect!"
10125{_}1235{_}I think I shall adopt your emendation in the "Dying Lover," though I do not myself feel the objection against "Silent Prayer."
10125{_}1236{_}My tailor has brought me home a new coat lapelled, with a velvet collar.
10125{_}1237{_}He assures me everybody wears velvet collars now.
10125{_}1238{_}Some are born fashionable, some achieve fashion, and others, like your humble servant, have fashion thrust upon them.
10125{_}1239{_}The rogue has been making inroads hitherto by modest degrees, foisting upon me an additional button, recommending gaiters; but to come upon me thus in a full tide of luxury, neither becomes him as a tailor or the ninth of a man.
