10125{_}3130{_}The nicest little rabbity things you ever tasted.
10125{_}3131{_}Do look about for them.
10125{_}3132{_}Make Mrs. Clare pick off the hind-quarters, boil them plain, with parsley and butter.
10125{_}3133{_}The fore-quarters are not so good.
10125{_}3134{_}She may let them hop off by themselves.
10125{_}3135{_}Yours sincerely,  CHAS.
10125{_}3136{_}LAMB.
10125{_}3137{_}[1] The Northamptonshire peasant poet.
10125{_}3138{_}He had sent Lamb his "The Village Minstrel, and other Poems."
10125{_}3139{_}LXX.
10125{_}3140{_}TO MR. BARRON FIELD.
10125{_}3141{_}_September_ 22, 1822.
10125{_}3142{_}My Dear F.,--I scribble hastily at office.
10125{_}3143{_}Frank wants my letter presently.
10125{_}3144{_}I and sister are just returned from Paris!
10125{_}3145{_}[1] We have eaten frogs.
10125{_}3146{_}It has been such a treat!
10125{_}3147{_}You know our monotonous general tenor.
10125{_}3148{_}Frogs are the nicest little delicate things,--rabbity flavored.
10125{_}3149{_}Imagine a Lilliputian rabbit!
10125{_}3150{_}They fricassee them; but in my mind, dressed seethed, plain, with parsley and butter, would have been the decision of Apicius.... Paris is a glorious, picturesque old city.
10125{_}3151{_}London looks mean and new to it, as the town of Washington would, seen after _it._ But they have no St. Paul's or Westminster Abbey.
10125{_}3152{_}The Seine, so much despised by Cockneys, is exactly the size to run through a magnificent street; palaces a mile long on one side, lofty Edinburgh stone (oh, the glorious antiques!)
10125{_}3153{_}houses on the other.
10125{_}3154{_}The Thames disunites London and Southwark.
10125{_}3155{_}I had Talma to supper with me.
10125{_}3156{_}He has picked up, as I believe, an authentic portrait of Shakspeare.
10125{_}3157{_}He paid a broker about L40 English for it.
10125{_}3158{_}It is painted on the one half of a pair of bellows,--a lovely picture, corresponding with the Folio head.
10125{_}3159{_}The bellows has old carved _wings_ round it and round the visnomy is inscribed, as near as I remember, not divided into rhyme,--I found out the rhyme,--    "Whom have we here   Stuck on this bellows,   But the Prince of good fellows,   Willy Shakspere?"
10125{_}3160{_}At top,--    "O base and coward lack,   To be here stuck!"
10125{_}3161{_}POINS.
10125{_}3162{_}At bottom,--    "Nay!
10125{_}3163{_}rather a glorious lot is to him assign'd,   Who, like the Almighty, rides upon the wind."
10125{_}3164{_}PISTOL,  This is all in old, carved wooden letters.
10125{_}3165{_}The countenance smiling, sweet, and intellectual beyond measure, even as he was immeasurable.
10125{_}3166{_}It may be a forgery.
10125{_}3167{_}They laugh at me, and tell me Ireland is in Paris, and has been putting off a portrait of the Black Prince.
10125{_}3168{_}How far old wood may be imitated I cannot say, Ireland was not found out by his parchments, but by his poetry.
10125{_}3169{_}I am confident no painter on either side the Channel could have painted anything near like the face I saw.
10125{_}3170{_}Again, would such a painter and forger have taken L40 for a thing, if authentic, worth L4000?
10125{_}3171{_}Talma is not in the secret, for he had not even found out the rhymes in the first inscription.
10125{_}3172{_}He is coming over with it, and my life to Southey's "Thalaba," it will gain universal faith.
10125{_}3173{_}The letter is wanted, and I am wanted.
10125{_}3174{_}Imagine the blank filled up with all kind things.
10125{_}3175{_}Our joint, hearty remembrances to both of you.
10125{_}3176{_}Yours as ever,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3177{_}[1] The Lambs had visited Paris on the invitation of James Kenney, the dramatist, who had married a Frenchwoman, and was living at Versailles.
10125{_}3178{_}LXXI.
10125{_}3179{_}TO WALTER WILSON.
10125{_}3180{_}_December_ 16, 1822.
10125{_}3181{_}Dear Wilson,--_Lightning_ I was going to call you.
10125{_}3182{_}You must have thought me negligent in not answering your letter sooner.
10125{_}3183{_}But I have a habit of never writing letters but at the office; 'tis so much time cribbed out of the Company; and I am but just got out of the thick of a tea-sale, in which most of the entry of notes, deposits, etc., usually falls to my share.
10125{_}3184{_}I have nothing of De Foe's but two or three novels and the "Plague History."
10125{_}3185{_}[1] I can give you no information about him.
10125{_}3186{_}As a slight general character of what I remember of them (for I have not looked into them latterly), I would say that in the appearance of _truth,_ in all the incidents and conversations that occur in them, they exceed any works of fiction I am acquainted with.
10125{_}3187{_}It is perfect illusion.
10125{_}3188{_}The _author_ never appears in these self-narratives (for so they ought to be called, or rather auto-biographies), but the _narrator_ chains us down to an implicit belief in everything he says.
10125{_}3189{_}There is all the minute detail of a log-book in it.
10125{_}3190{_}Dates are painfully pressed upon the memory.
10125{_}3191{_}Facts are repeated over and over in varying phrases, till you cannot choose but believe them.
10125{_}3192{_}It is like reading evidence given in a court of justice.
10125{_}3193{_}So anxious the story-teller seems that the truth should be clearly comprehended that when he has told us a matter of fact or a motive, in a line or two farther down he _repeats_ it with his favorite figure of speech, "I say" so and so, though he had made it abundantly plain before.
10125{_}3194{_}This is in imitation of the common people's way of speaking, or rather of the way in which they are addressed by a master or mistress who wishes to impress something upon their memories, and has a wonderful effect upon matter-of-fact readers.
10125{_}3195{_}Indeed, it is to such principally that he writes.
10125{_}3196{_}His style is everywhere beautiful, but plain and _homely._ "Robinson Crusoe" is delightful to all ranks and classes; but it is easy to see that it is written in phraseology peculiarly adapted to the lower conditions of readers,--hence it is an especial favorite with seafaring men, poor boys, servant-maids, etc.
10125{_}3197{_}His novels are capital kitchen-reading, while they are worthy, from their deep interest, to find a shelf in the libraries of the wealthiest and the most learned.
10125{_}3198{_}His passion for _matter-of-fact narrative_ sometimes betrayed him into a long relation of common incidents, which might happen to any man, and have no interest but the intense appearance of truth in them, to recommend them.
10125{_}3199{_}The whole latter half or two-thirds of "Colonel Jack" is of this description.
10125{_}3200{_}The beginning of "Colonel Jack" is the most affecting natural picture of a young thief that was ever drawn.
10125{_}3201{_}His losing the stolen money in the hollow of a tree, and finding it again when he was in despair, and then being in equal distress at not knowing how to dispose of it, and several similar touches in the early history of the Colonel, evince a deep knowledge of human nature, and putting out of question the superior _romantic_ interest of the latter, in my mind very much exceed "Crusoe."
10125{_}3202{_}"Roxana" (first edition) is the next in interest, though he left out the best part of it in subsequent editions from a foolish hypercriticism of his friend Southerne.
10125{_}3203{_}But "Moll Flanders," the "Account of the Plague," etc., are all of one family, and have the same stamp of character.
10125{_}3204{_}Believe me, with friendly recollections--Brother (as I used to call you), Yours,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3205{_}[1] Wilson was preparing a Life of De Foe, and had written to Lamb for guidance.
10125{_}3206{_}LXXII.
10125{_}3207{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3208{_}_December_ 23, 1822.
10125{_}3209{_}Dear Sir,--I have been so distracted with business and one thing or other, I have not had a quiet quarter of an hour for epistolary purposes.
10125{_}3210{_}Christmas, too, is come, which always puts a rattle into my morning skull.
10125{_}3211{_}It is a visiting, unquiet, unquakerish season.
10125{_}3212{_}I get more and more in love with solitude, and proportionately hampered with company.
10125{_}3213{_}I hope you have some holidays at this period.
10125{_}3214{_}I have one day,--Christmas Day; alas!
10125{_}3215{_}too few to commemorate the season.
10125{_}3216{_}All work and no play dulls me.
10125{_}3217{_}Company is not play, but many times bard work.
10125{_}3218{_}To play, is for a man to do what he pleases, or to do nothing,--to go about soothing his particular fancies.
10125{_}3219{_}I have lived to a time of life to have outlived the good hours, the nine-o'clock suppers, with a bright hour or two to clear up in afterwards.
10125{_}3220{_}Now you cannot get tea before that hour, and then sit gaping, music bothered perhaps, till half-past twelve brings up the tray; and what you steal of convivial enjoyment after, is heavily paid for in the disquiet of to-morrow's head.
10125{_}3221{_}I am pleased with your liking "John Woodvil," and amused with your knowledge of our drama being confined to Shakspeare and Miss Baillie.
10125{_}3222{_}What a world of fine territory between Land's End and Johnny Groat's have you missed traversing!
10125{_}3223{_}I could almost envy you to have so much to read.
10125{_}3224{_}I feel as if I had read all the books I want to read.
10125{_}3225{_}Oh, to forget Fielding, Steele, etc., and read 'em new!
10125{_}3226{_}Can you tell me a likely place where I could pick up cheap Fox's Journal?
10125{_}3227{_}There are no Quaker circulating libraries?
10125{_}3228{_}Elwood, too, I must have.
10125{_}3229{_}I rather grudge that Southey has taken up the history of your people; I am afraid he will put in some levity.
10125{_}3230{_}I am afraid I am not quite exempt from that fault in certain magazine articles, where I have introduced mention of them.
10125{_}3231{_}Were they to do again, I would reform them.
10125{_}3232{_}Why should not you write a poetical account of your old worthies, deducing them from Fox to Woolman?
10125{_}3233{_}But I remember you did talk of something of that kind, as a counterpart to the "Ecclesiastical Sketches."
10125{_}3234{_}But would not a poem be more consecutive than a string of sonnets?
10125{_}3235{_}You have no martyrs _quite to the fire,_ I think, among you, but plenty of heroic confessors, spirit-martyrs, lamb-lions.
10125{_}3236{_}Think of it; it would be better than a series of sonnets on "Eminent Bankers."
10125{_}3237{_}I like a hit at our way of life, though it does well for me,--better than anything short of _all one's time to one's self;_ for which alone I rankle with envy at the rich.
10125{_}3238{_}Books are good, and pictures are good, and money to buy them therefore good; but to buy _time,_--in other words, life!
10125{_}3239{_}The "compliments of the time" to you, should end my letter; to a Friend, I suppose, I must say the "sincerity of the season:" I hope they both mean the same.
10125{_}3240{_}With excuses for this hastily penned note, believe me, with great respect,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3241{_}LXXIII.
10125{_}3242{_}TO MISS WORDSWORTH.
10125{_}3243{_}Mary perfectly approves of the appropriation of the _feathers,_ and wishes them peacock's for your fair niece's sake.
10125{_}3244{_}_Christmas_, 1822.
10125{_}3245{_}Dear Miss Wordsworth,--I had just written the above endearing words when Monkhouse tapped me on the shoulder with an invitation to cold goose pie, which I was not bird of that sort enough to decline.
10125{_}3246{_}Mrs. Monkhouse, I am most happy to say, is better Mary has been tormented with a rheumatism, which is leaving her, I am suffering from the festivities of the season.
10125{_}3247{_}I wonder how my misused carcase holds it out.
10125{_}3248{_}I have played the experimental philosopher on it, that's certain.
10125{_}3249{_}Willy shall be welcome to a mince-pie and a bout at commerce whenever he comes.
10125{_}3250{_}He was in our eye.
10125{_}3251{_}I am glad you liked my new year's speculations; everybody likes them, except the author of the "Pleasures of Hope."
10125{_}3252{_}Disappointment attend him!
10125{_}3253{_}How I like to be liked, and _what I do_ to be liked!
10125{_}3254{_}They flatter me in magazines, newspapers, and all the minor reviews; the Quarterlies hold aloof.
10125{_}3255{_}But they must come into it in time, or their leaves be waste paper.
10125{_}3256{_}Salute Trinity Library in my name.
10125{_}3257{_}Two special things are worth seeing at Cambridge,--a portrait of Cromwell at Sidney, and a better of Dr. Harvey (who found out that blood was red) at Dr. Davy's; you should see them.
10125{_}3258{_}Coleridge is pretty well; I have not seen, him, but hear often of him, from Allsop, who sends me hares and pheasants twice a week; I can hardly take so fast as he gives.
10125{_}3259{_}I have almost forgotten butcher's meat as plebeian.
10125{_}3260{_}Are you not glad the cold is gone?
10125{_}3261{_}I find winters not so agreeable as they used to be "when winter bleak had charms forme," I cannot conjure up a kind similitude for those snowy flakes.
10125{_}3262{_}Let them keep to twelfth-cakes!
10125{_}3263{_}Mrs. Paris, our Cambridge friend, has been in town.
10125{_}3264{_}You do not know the Watfords in Trampington Street.
10125{_}3265{_}They are capital people.
10125{_}3266{_}Ask anybody you meet, who is the biggest woman in Cambridge, and I 'll hold you a wager they'll say Mrs. Smith; she broke down two benches in Trinity Gardens,--one on the confines of St. John's, which occasioned a litigation between the Societies as to repairing it.
10125{_}3267{_}In warm weather, she retires into an ice-cellar (literally!
10125{_}3268{_}), and dates the returns of the years from a hot Thursday some twenty years back.
10125{_}3269{_}She sits in a room with opposite doors and windows, to let in a thorough draught, which gives her slenderer friends tooth-aches.
10125{_}3270{_}She is to be seen in the market every morning at ten cheapening fowls, which I observe the Cambridge poulterers are not sufficiently careful to stump.
10125{_}3271{_}Having now answered most of the points contained in your letter, let me end with assuring you of our very best kindness, and excuse Mary for not handling the pen on this occasion, especially as it has fallen into so much better hands!
10125{_}3272{_}Will Dr. W. accept of my respects at the end of a foolish letter?
10125{_}3273{_}C. L.    LXXIV.
10125{_}3274{_}TO MR. AND MRS. BRUTON.
10125{_}3275{_}[1]  _January_ 6, 1823.
10125{_}3276{_}The pig was above my feeble praise.
10125{_}3277{_}It was a dear pigmy.
10125{_}3278{_}There was some contention as to who should have the ears; but in spite of his obstinacy (deaf as these little creatures are to advice), I contrived to get at one of them.
10125{_}3279{_}It came in boots, too, which I took as a favor.
10125{_}3280{_}Generally these petty-toes, pretty toes I are missing: but I suppose he wore them to look taller.
10125{_}3281{_}He must have been the least of his race.
10125{_}3282{_}His little foots would have gone into the silver slipper.
10125{_}3283{_}I take him to have beec a Chinese and a female.
10125{_}3284{_}If Evelyn could have seen him, he would never have farrowed two such prodigious volumes, seeing how much good can be contained in--how small a compass!
10125{_}3285{_}He crackled delicately.
10125{_}3286{_}I left a blank at the top of my letter, not being determined which to address it to j so farmer and farmer's wife will please to divide our thanks.
10125{_}3287{_}May your granaries be full, and your rats empty, and your chickens plump, and your envious neighbors lean, and your laborers busy, and you as idle and as happy as the day is long!
10125{_}3288{_}VIVE L'AGRICULTURE!
10125{_}3289{_}How do you make your pigs so little?
10125{_}3290{_}They are vastly engaging at the age.
10125{_}3291{_}I was so myself.
10125{_}3292{_}Now I am a disagreeable old hog,   A middle-aged gentleman-and-a-half;   My faculties (thank God!)
10125{_}3293{_}are not much impaired.
10125{_}3294{_}I have my sight, hearing, taste, pretty perfect, and can read the Lord's Prayer in common type, by the help of a candle, without making many mistakes....
10125{_}3295{_}Many happy returns, not of the pig, but of the New Year, to both.
10125{_}3296{_}Mary, for her share of the pig and the memoirs, desires to send the same.
10125{_}3297{_}Yours truly,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3298{_}[1] Hertfordshire connections of the Lambs.
10125{_}3299{_}LXXV.
10125{_}3300{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3301{_}[1]  _January_ 9, 1823.
10125{_}3302{_}Throw yourself on the world without any rational plan of support beyond what the chance employ of booksellers would afford you!
10125{_}3303{_}Throw yourself, rather, my dear sir, from the steep Tarpeian rock slap-dash headlong upon iron spikes.
10125{_}3304{_}If you had but five consolatory minutes between the desk and the bed, make much of them, and live a century in them, rather than turn slave to the booksellers.
10125{_}3305{_}They are Turks and Tartars when they have poor authors at their beck.
10125{_}3306{_}Hitherto you have been at arm's length from them.
10125{_}3307{_}Come not within their grasp.
10125{_}3308{_}I have known many authors want for bread, some repining, others envying the blessed security of a counting-house, all agreeing they had rather have been tailors, weavers,--what not,--rather than the things they were.
10125{_}3309{_}I have known some starved, some to go mad, one dear friend literally dying in a workhouse.
10125{_}3310{_}You know not what a rapacious, dishonest set these booksellers are.
10125{_}3311{_}Ask even Southey, who (a single case almost) has made a fortune by book-drudgery, what he has found them.
10125{_}3312{_}Oh, you know not--may you never know!--the miseries of subsisting by authorship.
10125{_}3313{_}'Tis a pretty appendage to a situation like yours or mine, but a slavery, worse than all slavery, to be a bookseller's dependant, to drudge your brains for pots of ale and breasts of mutton, to change your free thoughts and voluntary numbers for ungracious task-work.
10125{_}3314{_}Those fellows hate _us_.
10125{_}3315{_}The reason I take to be that, contrary to other trades, in which the master gets all the credit (a Jeweller or silversmith for instance), and the journeyman, who really does the fine work, is in the background, in _our_ work the world gives all the credit to us, whom _they_ consider as _their_ journeymen, and therefore do they hate us, and cheat us, and oppress us, and would wring the blood of as out, to put another sixpence in their mechanic pouches!
10125{_}3316{_}I contend that a bookseller has a _relative honesty_ towards authors, not like his honesty to the rest of the world.
10125{_}3317{_}Baldwin, who first engaged me as Elia, has not paid me up yet (nor any of us without repeated mortifying appeals).
10125{_}3318{_}Yet how the knave fawned when I was of service to him!
10125{_}3319{_}Yet I daresay the fellow is punctual in settling his milk-score, etc.
10125{_}3320{_}Keep to your bank, and the bank will keep you.
10125{_}3321{_}Trust not to the public; you may hang, starve, drown yourself, for anything that worthy _personage_ cares.
10125{_}3322{_}I bless every star that Providence, not seeing good to make me independent, has seen it next good to settle me upon the stable foundation of Leadenhall.
10125{_}3323{_}Sit down, good B.B., in the banking-office; what!
10125{_}3324{_}is there not from six to eleven P.M. six days in the week, and is there not all Sunday?
10125{_}3325{_}Fie!
10125{_}3326{_}what a superfluity of man's time, if you could think so,--enough for relaxation, mirth, converse, poetry, good thoughts, quiet thoughts.
10125{_}3327{_}Oh, the corroding, torturing, tormenting thoughts that disturb the brain of the unlucky wight who must draw upon it for daily sustenance!
10125{_}3328{_}Henceforth I retract all my foul complaints of mercantile employment; look upon them as lovers' quarrels.
10125{_}3329{_}I was but half in earnest.
10125{_}3330{_}Welcome, dead timber of a desk, that makes me live!
10125{_}3331{_}A little grumbling is a wholesome medicine for the spleen, but in my inner heart do I approve and embrace this our close, but unharassing, way of life.
10125{_}3332{_}I am quite serious.
10125{_}3333{_}If you can send me Fox, I will not keep it _six weeks_, and will return it, with warm thanks to yourself and friend, without blot or dog's-ear.
10125{_}3334{_}You will much oblige me by this kindness.
10125{_}3335{_}Yours truly,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3336{_}[1] The Quaker poet.
10125{_}3337{_}Mr. Barton was a clerk in the bank of the Messrs. Alexander, of Woodbridge, in Suffolk.
10125{_}3338{_}Encouraged by his literary success, he thought of throwing up his clerkship and trusting to his pen for a livelihood,--a design from which he was happily diverted by his friends.
10125{_}3339{_}LXXVI.
10125{_}3340{_}TO MISS HUTCHINSON.
10125{_}3341{_}_April_ 25, 1823.
10125{_}3342{_}Dear Miss H.,--Mary has such an invincible reluctance to any epistolary exertion that I am sparing her a mortification by taking the pen from her.
10125{_}3343{_}The plain truth is, she writes such a mean, detestable hand that she is ashamed of the formation of her letters.
10125{_}3344{_}There is an essential poverty and abjectness in the frame of them.
10125{_}3345{_}They look like begging letters.
10125{_}3346{_}And then she is sure to omit a most substantial word in the second draught (for she never ventures an epistle without a foul copy first), which is obliged to be interlined,--which spoils the neatest epistle, you know.
10125{_}3347{_}Her figures, 1, 2, 3, 4, etc., where she has occasion to express numerals, as in the date (25th April, 1823), are not figures, but figurantes; and the combined posse go staggering up and down shameless, as drunkards in the daytime.
10125{_}3348{_}It is no better when she rules her paper.
10125{_}3349{_}Her lines "are not less erring" than her words; a sort of unnatural parallel lines, that are perpetually threatening to meet,--which, you know, is quite contrary to Euclid.
10125{_}3350{_}Her very blots are not bold, like this [_here a large blot is inserted_], but poor smears, half left in and half scratched out, with another smear left in their place.
10125{_}3351{_}I like a clear letter; a bold, free hand and a fearless flourish.
10125{_}3352{_}Then she has always to go through them (a second operation) to dot her _i_'s and cross her _t_'s.
10125{_}3353{_}I don't think she could make a corkscrew if she tried,--which has such a fine effect at the end or middle of an epistle, and fills up.
10125{_}3354{_}There is a corkscrew!
10125{_}3355{_}One of the best I ever drew.
10125{_}3356{_}[1] By the way, what incomparable whiskey that was of Monkhouse's!
10125{_}3357{_}But if I am to write a letter, let me begin, and not stand flourishing like a fencer at a fair.
10125{_}3358{_}_April_ 25, 1823.
10125{_}3359{_}Dear Miss H.,--It gives me great pleasure [the letter now begins] to hear that you got down so smoothly, and that Mrs. Monkhouse's spirits are so good and enterprising.
10125{_}3360{_}[2] It shows, whatever her posture may be, that her mind at least is not supine.
10125{_}3361{_}I hope the excursion will enable the former to keep pace with its outstripping neighbor.
10125{_}3362{_}Pray present our kindest wishes to her and all (that sentence should properly have come into the postscript; but we airy, mercurial spirits, there is no keeping us in).
10125{_}3363{_}"Time" (as was said of one of us) "toils after us in vain."
10125{_}3364{_}I am afraid our co-visit with Coleridge was a dream.
10125{_}3365{_}I shall not get away before the end or middle of June, and then you will be frog-hopping at Boulogne.
10125{_}3366{_}And besides, I think the Gilmans would scarce trust him with us; I have a malicious knack at cutting of apron-strings.
10125{_}3367{_}The saints' days you speak of have long since fled to heaven with Astraea, and the cold piety of the age lacks fervor to recall them; only Peter left his key,--the iron one of the two that "shuts amain,"--and that is the reason I am locked up.
10125{_}3368{_}Meanwhile, of afternoons we pick up primroses at Dalston, and Mary corrects me when I call 'em cowslips.
10125{_}3369{_}God bless you all, and pray remember me euphoniously to Mr. Gruvellegan.
10125{_}3370{_}That Lee Priory must be a dainty bower.
10125{_}3371{_}Is it built of flints?
10125{_}3372{_}and does it stand at Kingsgate?
10125{_}3373{_}[1] Lamb was fond of this flourish, and it is frequently found in his letters.
10125{_}3374{_}[2] Miss Hutchinson's invalid relative.
10125{_}3375{_}LXXVII.
10125{_}3376{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3377{_}_September_ 2, 1823.
10125{_}3378{_}Dear B.B.,--What will you not say to my not writing?
10125{_}3379{_}You cannot say I do not write now.
10125{_}3380{_}Hessey has not used your kind sonnet, nor have I seen it.
10125{_}3381{_}Pray send me a copy.
10125{_}3382{_}Neither have I heard any more of your friend's MS., which I will reclaim whenever you please.
10125{_}3383{_}When you come Londonward, you will find me no longer in Covent Garden: I have a cottage in Colebrook Row, Islington,--a cottage, for it is detached; a white house, with six good rooms, The New River (rather elderly by this time) runs (if a moderate walking pace can be so termed) close to the foot of the house; and behind is a spacious garden with vines (I assure you), pears, strawberries, parsnips, leeks, carrots, cabbages, to delight the heart of old Alcinous.
10125{_}3384{_}You enter without passage into a cheerful dining-room, all studded over and rough with old books; and above is a lightsome drawing-room, three windows, full of choice prints.
10125{_}3385{_}I feel like a great lord, never having had a house before.
10125{_}3386{_}The "London," I fear, falls off.
10125{_}3387{_}I linger among its creaking rafters, like the last rat; it will topple down if they don't get some buttresses.
10125{_}3388{_}They have pulled down three,--Hazlitt, Procter, and their best stay, kind, light-hearted Wainewright, their Janus.
10125{_}3389{_}[1] The best is, neither of our fortunes is concerned in it.
10125{_}3390{_}I heard of you from Mr. Pulham this morning, and that gave a fillip to my laziness, which has been intolerable; but I am so taken up with pruning and gardening,--quite a new sort of occupation to me.
10125{_}3391{_}I have gathered my jargonels; but my Windsor pears are backward.
10125{_}3392{_}The former were of exquisite raciness.
10125{_}3393{_}I do now sit under my own vine, and contemplate the growth of vegetable nature.
10125{_}3394{_}I can now understand in what sense they speak of father Adam.
10125{_}3395{_}I recognize the paternity while I watch my tulips.
10125{_}3396{_}I almost fell with him, for the first day I turned a drunken gardener (as he let in the serpent) into my Eden; and he laid about him, lopping off some choice boughs, etc., which hung over from a neighbor's garden, and in his blind zeal laid waste a shade which had sheltered their window from the gaze of passers-by.
10125{_}3397{_}The old gentlewoman (fury made her not handsome) could scarcely be reconciled by all my fine words.
10125{_}3398{_}There was no buttering her parsnips.
10125{_}3399{_}She talked of the law.
10125{_}3400{_}What a lapse to commit on the first day of my happy "garden state"!
10125{_}3401{_}I hope you transmitted the Fox-Journal to its owner, with suitable thanks.
10125{_}3402{_}Mr. Cary, the Dante man, dines with me to-day.
10125{_}3403{_}He is a mode of a country parson, lean (as a curate ought to be), modest, sensible, no obtruder of church dogmas, quite a different man from Southey.
10125{_}3404{_}You would like him.
10125{_}3405{_}Pray accept this for a letter, and believe me, with sincere regards, yours,  C.L.
10125{_}3406{_}[1] Wainewright, the notorious poisoner, who, under the name of "Janus Weathercock," contributed various frothy papers on art and literature to the "London Magazine."
10125{_}3407{_}LXXVIII.
10125{_}3408{_}TO MRS. HAZLITT.
10125{_}3409{_}_November_, 1823.
10125{_}3410{_}Dear Mrs. H.,--Sitting down to write a letter is such a painful operation to Mary that you must accept me as her proxy.
10125{_}3411{_}You have seen our house.
10125{_}3412{_}What I now tell you is literally true.
10125{_}3413{_}Yesterday week, George Dyer called upon us, at one o'clock (_bright noonday_), on his way to dine with Mrs. Barbauld at Newington.
10125{_}3414{_}He sat with Mary about half an hour, and took leave.
10125{_}3415{_}The maid saw him go out from her kitchen window, but suddenly losing sight of him, ran up in a fright to Mary.
10125{_}3416{_}G.D., instead of keeping the slip that leads to the gate, had deliberately, staff in hand, in broad, open day, marched into the New River.
10125{_}3417{_}[1] He had not his spectacles on, and you know his absence.
10125{_}3418{_}Who helped him out, they can hardly tell; but between 'em they got him out, drenched thro' and thro'.
10125{_}3419{_}A mob collected by that time, and accompanied him in.
10125{_}3420{_}"Send for the doctor!"
10125{_}3421{_}they said; and a one-eyed fellow, dirty and drunk, was fetched from the public-house at the end, where it seem he lurks for the sake of picking up water-practice, having formerly had a medal from the Humane Society for some rescue.
10125{_}3422{_}By his advice the patient was put between blankets; and when I came home at four to dinner, I found G.D. a-bed, and raving, light-headed with the brandy-and-water which the doctor had administered.
10125{_}3423{_}He sang, laughed, whimpered, screamed, babbled of guardian angels, would get up and go home; but we kept him there by force; and by next morning he departed sobered, and seems to have received no injury.
10125{_}3424{_}[2] All my friends are open-mouthed about having paling before the river; but I cannot see that because a ... lunatic chooses to walk into a river, with his eyes open, at mid-day, I am any the more likely to be drowned in it, coming home at midnight.
10125{_}3425{_}[1] See Elia-essay, "Amicus Redivivus."
10125{_}3426{_}[2] In the "Athenaeum" for 1835 Procter says: "I happened to call at Lamb's house about ten minutes after this accident; I saw before me a train of water running from the door to the river.
10125{_}3427{_}Lamb had gone for a surgeon; the maid was running about distraught, with dry clothes on one arm, and the dripping habiliments of the involuntary bather in the other.
10125{_}3428{_}Miss Lamb, agitated, and whimpering forth 'Poor Mr.
10125{_}3429{_}Dyer!'
10125{_}3430{_}in the most forlorn voice, stood plunging her hands into the wet pockets of his trousers, to fish up the wet coin.
10125{_}3431{_}Dyer himself, an amiable little old man, who took water _in_ternally and eschewed strong liquors, lay on his host's bed, hidden by blankets; his head, on which was his short gray hair, alone peered out; and this, having been rubbed dry by a resolute hand,--by the maid's, I believe, who assisted at the rescue,--looked as if bristling with a thousand needles.
10125{_}3432{_}Lamb, moreover, in his anxiety, had administered a formidable dose of cognac and water to the sufferer, and _he_ (used only to the simple element) babbled without cessation."
10125{_}3433{_}LXXIX.
10125{_}3434{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3435{_}_January_ 9, 1824.
10125{_}3436{_}Dear B.B.,--Do you know what it is to succumb under an insurmountable day-mare,--"a whoreson lethargy," Falstaff calls it,--an indisposition to do anything or to be anything; a total deadness and distaste; a suspension of vitality; an indifference to locality; a numb, soporifical good-for-nothingness; an ossification all over; an oyster-like insensibility to the passing events; a mind-stupor; a brawny defiance to the needles of a thrusting-in conscience?
10125{_}3437{_}Did you ever have a very bad cold, with a total irresolution to submit to water-gruel processes?
10125{_}3438{_}This has been for many weeks my lot and my excuse.
10125{_}3439{_}My fingers drag heavily over this paper, and to my thinking it is three-and-twenty furlongs from here to the end of this demi-sheet.
10125{_}3440{_}I have not a thing to say; nothing is of more importance than another.
10125{_}3441{_}I am flatter than a denial or a pancake; emptier than Judge Parke's wig when the head is in it; duller than a country stage when the actors are off it,--a cipher, an o!
10125{_}3442{_}I acknowledge life at all only by an occasional convulsional cough and a permanent phlegmatic pain in the chest.
10125{_}3443{_}I am weary of the world; life is weary of me, My day is gone into twilight, and I don't think it worth the expense of candles.
10125{_}3444{_}My wick hath a thief in it, but I can't muster courage to snuff it.
10125{_}3445{_}I inhale suffocation; I can't distinguish veal from mutton; nothing interests me.
10125{_}3446{_}'T is twelve o'clock, and Thurtell [1] is just now coming out upon the new drop, Jack Ketch alertly tucking up his greasy sleeves to do the last office of mortality; yet cannot I elicit a groan or a moral reflection.
10125{_}3447{_}If you told me the world will be at an end to-morrow, I should just say, "Will it?"
10125{_}3448{_}I have not volition enough left to dot my _i_'s, much less to comb my eyebrows; my eyes are set in my head; my brains are gone out to see a poor relation in Moorfields, and they did not say when they'd come back again; my skull is a Grub Street attic to let,--not so much as a joint-stool left in it; my hand writes, not I, from habit, as chickens run about a little when their heads are off.
10125{_}3449{_}Oh for a vigorous fit of gout, colic, toothache,--an earwig in my auditory, a fly in my visual organs; pain is life,--the sharper the more evidence of life; but this apathy, this death!
10125{_}3450{_}Did you ever have an obstinate cold,--a six or seven weeks' unintermitting chill and suspension of hope, fear, conscience, and everything?
10125{_}3451{_}Yet do I try all I can to cure it.
10125{_}3452{_}I try wine, and spirits, and smoking, and snuff in unsparing quantities; but they all only seem to make me worse, instead of better.
10125{_}3453{_}I sleep in a damp room, but it does me no good; I come home late o' nights, but do not find any visible amendment!
10125{_}3454{_}Who shall deliver me from the body of this death?
10125{_}3455{_}It is just fifteen minutes after twelve.
10125{_}3456{_}Thurtell is by this time a good way on his journey, baiting at Scorpion, perhaps.
10125{_}3457{_}Ketch is bargaining for his cast coat and waistcoat; and the Jew demurs at first at three half-crowns, but on consideration that he may get somewhat by showing 'em in the town, finally closes.
10125{_}3458{_}C. L.  [1] Hanged that day for the murder of Weare.
10125{_}3459{_}LXXX.
10125{_}3460{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3461{_}_January_ 23, 1824.
10125{_}3462{_}My dear sir,--That peevish letter of mine, [1] which was meant to convey an apology for my incapacity to write, seems to have been taken by you in too serious a light,--it was only my way of telling you I had a severe cold.
10125{_}3463{_}The fact is, I have been insuperably dull and lethargic for many weeks, and cannot rise to the vigor of a letter, much less an essay.
10125{_}3464{_}The "London" must do without me for a time, for I have lost all interest about it; and whether I shall recover it again I know not.
10125{_}3465{_}I will bridle my pen another time, and not tease and puzzle you with my aridities.
10125{_}3466{_}I shall begin to feel a little more alive with the spring.
10125{_}3467{_}Winter is to me (mild or harsh) always a great trial of the spirits.
10125{_}3468{_}I am ashamed not to have noticed your tribute to Woolman, whom we love so much; it is done in your good manner.
10125{_}3469{_}Your friend Tayler called upon me some time since, and seems a very amiable man.
10125{_}3470{_}His last story is painfully fine.
10125{_}3471{_}His book I "like;" it is only too stuffed with Scripture, too parsonish.
10125{_}3472{_}The best thing in it is the boy's own story.
10125{_}3473{_}When I say it is too full of Scripture, I mean it is too full of direct quotations; no book can have too much of silent Scripture in it.
10125{_}3474{_}But the natural power of a story is diminished when the uppermost purpose in the writer seems to be to recommend something else,--namely, Religion.
10125{_}3475{_}You know what Horace says of the _Deus intersit_?
10125{_}3476{_}I am not able to explain myself,--you must do it for me.
10125{_}3477{_}My sister's part in the "Leicester School" (about two thirds) was purely her own; as it was (to the same quantity) in the "Shakspeare Tales" which bear my name.
10125{_}3478{_}I wrote only the "Witch Aunt," the "First Going to Church," and the final story about "A little Indian girl" in a ship.
10125{_}3479{_}Your account of my black-balling amused me.
10125{_}3480{_}_I think, as Quakers, they did right._ There are some things hard to be understood.
10125{_}3481{_}The more I think, the more I am vexed at having puzzled you with that letter; but I have been so out of letter-writing of late years that it is a sore effort to sit down to it; and I felt in your debt, and sat down waywardly to pay you in bad money.
10125{_}3482{_}Never mind my dulness; I am used to long intervals of it.
10125{_}3483{_}The heavens seem brass to me; then again comes the refreshing shower,--    "I have been merry twice and once ere now."
10125{_}3484{_}You said something about Mr. Mitford in a late letter, which I believe I did not advert to.
10125{_}3485{_}I shall be happy to show him my Milton (it is all the show things I have) at any time he will take the trouble of a jaunt to Islington.
10125{_}3486{_}I do also hope to see Mr. Tayler there some day.
10125{_}3487{_}Pray say so to both.
10125{_}3488{_}Coleridge's book is in good part printed, but sticks a little for _more copy_.
10125{_}3489{_}It bears an unsalable title,--"Extracts from Bishop Leighton;" but I am confident there will be plenty of good notes in it,--more of Bishop Coleridge than Leighton in it, I hope; for what is Leighton?
10125{_}3490{_}Do you trouble yourself about libel cases?
10125{_}3491{_}The decision against Hunt for the "Vision of Judgment" made me sick.
10125{_}3492{_}What is to become of the good old talk about our good old king,--his personal virtues saving us from a revolution, etc.?
10125{_}3493{_}Why, none that think can utter it now.
10125{_}3494{_}It must stink.
10125{_}3495{_}And the "Vision" is as to himward such a tolerant, good-humored thing!
10125{_}3496{_}What a wretched thing a Lord Chief Justice is, always was, and will be!
10125{_}3497{_}Keep your good spirits up, dear B.
10125{_}3498{_}B., mine will return; they are at present in abeyance, but I am rather lethargic than miserable.
10125{_}3499{_}I don't know but a good horsewhip would be more beneficial to me than physic.
10125{_}3500{_}My head, without aching, will teach yours to ache.
10125{_}3501{_}It is well I am getting to the conclusion.
10125{_}3502{_}I will send a better letter when I am a better man.
10125{_}3503{_}Let me thank you for your kind concern for me (which I trust will have reason soon to be dissipated), and assure you that it gives me pleasure to hear from you.
10125{_}3504{_}Yours truly,  C. L.  [1] Letter LXXIX.
10125{_}3505{_}LXXXI.
10125{_}3506{_}TO BERNARD BARTON  _April_, 1824.
10125{_}3507{_}Dear B.B.,--I am sure I cannot fill a letter, though I should disfurnish my skull to fill it; but you expect something, and shall have a notelet.
10125{_}3508{_}Is Sunday, not divinely speaking, but humanly and holiday-sically, a blessing?
10125{_}3509{_}Without its institution, would our rugged taskmasters have given us a leisure day so often, think you, as once in a month?
10125{_}3510{_}or, if it had not been instituted, might they not have given us every sixth day?
10125{_}3511{_}Solve me this problem.
10125{_}3512{_}If we are to go three times a-day to church, why has Sunday slipped into the notion of a _holi_day?
10125{_}3513{_}A HOLY-day, I grant it.
10125{_}3514{_}The Puritans, I have read in Southey's book, knew the distinction.
10125{_}3515{_}They made people observe Sunday rigorously, would not let a nurserymaid walk out in the fields with children for recreation on that day.
10125{_}3516{_}But _then_ they gave the people a holiday from all sorts of work every second Tuesday.
10125{_}3517{_}This was giving to the two Caesars that which was _his_ respective.
10125{_}3518{_}Wise, beautiful, thoughtful, generous legislators!
10125{_}3519{_}Would Wilberforce give us our Tuesdays?
10125{_}3520{_}No; he would turn the six days into sevenths,--    "And those three smiling seasons of the year   Into a Russian winter."
10125{_}3521{_}OLD PLAY.
10125{_}3522{_}I am sitting opposite a person who is making strange distortions with the gout, which is not unpleasant pleasant,--to me, at least.
10125{_}3523{_}What is the reason we do not sympathize with pain, short of some terrible surgical operation?
10125{_}3524{_}Hazlitt, who boldly says all he feels, avows that not only he does not pity sick people, but he hates them.
10125{_}3525{_}I obscurely recognize his meaning.
10125{_}3526{_}Pain is probably too selfish a consideration, too simply a consideration of self-attention.
10125{_}3527{_}We pity poverty, loss of friends, etc.,--more complex things, in which the sufferer's feelings are associated with others.
10125{_}3528{_}This is a rough thought suggested by the presence of gout; I want head to extricate it and plane it.
10125{_}3529{_}What is all this to your letter?
10125{_}3530{_}I felt it to be a good one, but my turn, when I write at all, is perversely to travel out of the record, so that my letters are anything but answers.
10125{_}3531{_}So you still want a motto?
10125{_}3532{_}You must not take my ironical one, because your book, I take it, is too serious for it.
10125{_}3533{_}Bickerstaff might have used it for _his_ lucubrations.
10125{_}3534{_}What do you think of (for a title) Religio Tremuli?
10125{_}3535{_}or Tremebundi?
10125{_}3536{_}There is Religio Medici and Laici.
10125{_}3537{_}But perhaps the volume is not quite Quakerish enough, or exclusively so, for it.
10125{_}3538{_}Your own "Vigils" is perhaps the best.
10125{_}3539{_}While I have space, let me congratulate with you the return of spring,--what a summery spring too!
10125{_}3540{_}All those qualms about the dog and cray-fish [1] melt before it.
10125{_}3541{_}I am going to be happy and _vain_ again.
10125{_}3542{_}A hasty farewell,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3543{_}[1] Lamb had confessed, in a previous letter to Barton, to having once wantonly set a dog upon a cray-fish.
10125{_}3544{_}LXXXII.
10125{_}3545{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3546{_}_May_ 15, 1824.
10125{_}3547{_}Dear B.
10125{_}3548{_}B.,--I am oppressed with business all day, and company all night.
10125{_}3549{_}But I will snatch a quarter of an hour.
10125{_}3550{_}Your recent acquisitions of the picture and the letter are greatly to be congratulated.
10125{_}3551{_}I too have a picture of my father and the copy of his first love-verses; but they have been mine long.
10125{_}3552{_}Blake is a real name, I assure you, and a most extraordinary man, if he is still living.
10125{_}3553{_}He is the Robert [William] Blake whose wild designs accompany a splendid folio edition of the "Night Thoughts," which you may have seen, in one of which he pictures the parting of soul and body by a solid mass of human form floating off, God knows how, from a lumpish mass (fac-simile to itself) left behind on the dying bed.
10125{_}3554{_}He paints in water-colors marvellous strange pictures, visions of his brain, which he asserts that he has seen; they have great merit.
10125{_}3555{_}He has _seen_ the old Welsh bards on Snowdon,--he has seen the beautifullest, the strongest, and the ugliest man, left alone from the massacre of the Britons by the Romans, and has painted them from memory (I have seen his paintings), and asserts them to be as good as the figures of Raphael and Angelo, but not better, as they had precisely the same retro-visions and prophetic visions with themself [himself].
10125{_}3556{_}The painters in oil (which he will have it that neither of them practised) he affirms to have been the ruin of art, and affirms that all the while he was engaged in his Welsh paintings, Titian was disturbing him,-- Titian the Ill Genius of Oil Painting.
10125{_}3557{_}His pictures--one in particular, the Canterbury Pilgrims, far above Stothard--have great merit, but hard, dry, yet with grace.
10125{_}3558{_}He has written a Catalogue of them, with a most spirited criticism on Chaucer, but mystical and full of vision.
10125{_}3559{_}His poems have been sold hitherto only in manuscript.
10125{_}3560{_}I never read them; but a friend at my desire procured the "Sweep Song."
10125{_}3561{_}There is one to a tiger, which I have heard recited, beginning,--    "Tiger, Tiger, burning bright,   Thro' the deserts of the night,"  which is glorious, but, alas!
10125{_}3562{_}I have not the book; for the man is flown, whither I know not,--to Hades or a madhouse.
10125{_}3563{_}But I must look on him as one of the most extraordinary persons of the age.
10125{_}3564{_}Montgomery's book [1] I have not much hope from, and the society with the affected name [2] has been laboring at it for these twenty years, and made few converts.
10125{_}3565{_}I think it was injudicious to mix stories, avowedly colored by fiction, with the sad, true statements from the parliamentary records, etc.
10125{_}3566{_}But I wish the little negroes all the good that can come from it.
10125{_}3567{_}I battered my brains (not buttered them,--but it is a bad _a_) for a few verses for them, but I could make nothing of it.
10125{_}3568{_}You have been luckier.
10125{_}3569{_}But Blake's are the flower of the set, you will, I am sure, agree; though some of Montgomery's at the end are pretty, but the Dream awkwardly paraphrased from B.
10125{_}3570{_}With the exception of an Epilogue for a Private Theatrical, I have written nothing new for near six months.
10125{_}3571{_}It is in vain to spur me on.
10125{_}3572{_}I must wait.
10125{_}3573{_}I cannot write without a genial impulse, and I have none.
10125{_}3574{_}'T is barren all and dearth.
10125{_}3575{_}No matter; life is something without scribbling.
10125{_}3576{_}I have got rid of my bad spirits, and hold up pretty well this rain-damned May.
10125{_}3577{_}So we have lost another poet.
10125{_}3578{_}[3] I never much relished his Lordship's mind, and shall be sorry if the Greeks have cause to miss him.
10125{_}3579{_}He was to me offensive, and I never can make out his real _power_, which his admirers talk of.
10125{_}3580{_}Why, a, line of Wordsworth's is a lever to lift the immortal spirit; Byron can only move the spleen.
10125{_}3581{_}He was at best a satirist.
10125{_}3582{_}In any other way, he was mean enough.
10125{_}3583{_}I daresay I do him injustice; but I cannot love him, nor squeeze a tear to his memory.
10125{_}3584{_}He did not like the world, and he has left it, as Alderman Curtis advised the Radicals, "if they don't like their country, damn 'em, let 'em leave it," they possessing no rood of ground in England, and he ten thousand acres.
10125{_}3585{_}Byron was better than many Curtises.
10125{_}3586{_}Farewell, and accept this apology for a letter from one who owes you so much in that kind.
10125{_}3587{_}Yours ever truly, C. L.  [1] "The Chimney-Sweeper's Friend, and Climbing-Boy's Album,"--a book, by James Montgomery, setting forth the wrongs of the little chimney-sweepers, for whose relief a society had been started.
10125{_}3588{_}[2] The Society for Ameliorating the Condition of Infant Chimney-Sweepers.
10125{_}3589{_}[3] Byron had died on April 19.
10125{_}3590{_}LXXXIII.
10125{_}3591{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3592{_}_August_, 1824.
10125{_}3593{_}I can no more understand Shelley than you can; his poetry is "thin sown with profit or delight."
10125{_}3594{_}Yet I must point to your notice a sonnet conceived and expressed with a witty delicacy.
10125{_}3595{_}It is that addressed to one who hated him, but who could not persuade him to hate _him_ again.
10125{_}3596{_}His coyness to the other's passion--for hate demands a return as much as love, and starves without it--is most arch and pleasant.
10125{_}3597{_}Pray, like it very much.
10125{_}3598{_}For his theories and nostrums, they are oracular enough, but I either comprehend 'em not, or there is "miching malice" and mischief in 'em, but, for the most part, ringing with their own emptiness.
10125{_}3599{_}Hazlitt said well of 'em: "Many are the wiser and better for reading Shakspeare, but nobody was ever wiser or better for reading Shelley."
10125{_}3600{_}I wonder you will sow your correspondence on so barren a ground as I am, that make such poor returns.
10125{_}3601{_}But my head aches at the bare thought of letter-writing.
10125{_}3602{_}I wish all the ink in the ocean dried up, and would listen to the quills shivering up in the candle flame, like parching martyrs.
10125{_}3603{_}The same indisposition to write it is has stopped my "Elias;" but you will see a futile effort in the next number, [1] "wrung from me with slow pain."
10125{_}3604{_}The fact is, my head is seldom cool enough.
10125{_}3605{_}I am dreadfully indolent.
10125{_}3606{_}To have to do anything--to order me a new coat, for instance, though my old buttons are shelled like beans--is an effort.
10125{_}3607{_}My pen stammers like my tongue.
10125{_}3608{_}What cool craniums those old inditers of folios must have had, what a mortified pulse!
10125{_}3609{_}Well, once more I throw myself on your mercy.
10125{_}3610{_}Wishing peace in thy new dwelling,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3611{_}[1] The essay "Blakesmoor in Hertfordshire," in the "London Magazine" for September, 1824.
10125{_}3612{_}LXXXIV.
10125{_}3613{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3614{_}_December_ 1, 1824.
10125{_}3615{_}Taylor and Hessey, finding their magazine [1] goes off very heavily at 2_s_.
10125{_}3616{_}6_d_., are prudently going to raise their price another shilling; and having already more authors than they want, intend to increase the number of them.
10125{_}3617{_}If they set up against the "New Monthly," they must change their present hands.
10125{_}3618{_}It is not tying the dead carcase of a review to a half-dead magazine will do their business.
10125{_}3619{_}It is like George Dyer multiplying his volumes to make 'em sell better.
10125{_}3620{_}When he finds one will not go off, he publishes two; two stick, he tries three; three hang fire, he is confident that four will have a better chance.
10125{_}3621{_}And now, my dear sir, trifling apart, the gloomy catastrophe of yesterday morning prompts a sadder vein.
10125{_}3622{_}The fate of the unfortunate Fauntleroy [2] makes me, whether I will or no, to cast reflecting eyes around on such of my friends as, by a parity of situation, are exposed to a similarity of temptation.
10125{_}3623{_}My very style seems to myself to become more impressive than usual, with the change of theme.
10125{_}3624{_}Who, that standeth, knoweth but he may yet fall?
10125{_}3625{_}Your hands as yet, I am most willing to believe, have never deviated, into others' property; you think it impossible that you could ever commit so heinous an offence.
10125{_}3626{_}But so thought Fauntleroy once; so have thought many besides him, who at last have expiated as he hath done.
10125{_}3627{_}You are as yet upright; but you are a banker,--at least, the next thing to it.
10125{_}3628{_}I feel the delicacy of the subject; but cash must pass through your hands, sometimes to a great amount.
10125{_}3629{_}If in an unguarded hour--But I will hope better.
10125{_}3630{_}Consider the scandal it will bring upon those of your persuasion.
10125{_}3631{_}Thousands would go to see a Quaker hanged, that would be indifferent to the fate of a Presbyterian or an Anabaptist.
10125{_}3632{_}Think of the effect it would have on the sale of your poems alone, not to mention higher considerations!
10125{_}3633{_}I tremble, I am sure, at myself, when I think that so many poor victims of the law, at one time of their life, made as sure of never being hanged as I, in my presumption, am too ready to do myself.
10125{_}3634{_}What are we better than they?
10125{_}3635{_}Do we come into the world with different necks?
10125{_}3636{_}Is there any distinctive mark under our left ears?
10125{_}3637{_}Are we unstrangulable, I ask you?
10125{_}3638{_}Think of these things.
10125{_}3639{_}I am shocked sometimes at the shape of my own fingers, not for their resemblance to the ape tribe (which is something), but for the exquisite adaptation of them to the purposes of picking fingering, etc.
10125{_}3640{_}No one that is so framed, I maintain it, but should tremble.
10125{_}3641{_}C. L.  [1] Taylor and Hessey succeeded John Scott as editors of the "London Magazine" (of which they were also publishers), and it was to this periodical that most of Lamb's Elia Essays were contributed.
10125{_}3642{_}[2] The forger, hanged Nov. 30, 1824.
10125{_}3643{_}This was the last execution for this offence.
10125{_}3644{_}LXXXV.
10125{_}3645{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3646{_}_March_ 23, 1825.
10125{_}3647{_}Dear B.
10125{_}3648{_}B.,--I have had no impulse to write, or attend to any single object but myself for weeks past,--my single self, I by myself, I. I am sick of hope deferred.
10125{_}3649{_}The grand wheel is in agitation that is to turn up my fortune; but round it rolls, and will turn up nothing.
10125{_}3650{_}I have a glimpse of freedom, of becoming a gentleman at large; but I am put off from day to day.
10125{_}3651{_}I have offered my resignation, and it is neither accepted nor rejected.
10125{_}3652{_}Eight weeks am I kept in this fearful suspense.
10125{_}3653{_}Guess what an absorbing stake I feel it.
10125{_}3654{_}I am not conscious of the existence of friends present or absent.
10125{_}3655{_}The East India Directors alone can be that thing to me or not.
10125{_}3656{_}I have just learned that nothing will be decided this week.
10125{_}3657{_}Why the next?
10125{_}3658{_}Why any week?
10125{_}3659{_}It has fretted me into an itch of the fingers; I rub 'em against paper, and write to you, rather than not allay this scorbuta.
10125{_}3660{_}While I can write, let me adjure you to have no doubts of Irving.
10125{_}3661{_}Let Mr. Mitford drop his disrespect.
10125{_}3662{_}Irving has prefixed a dedication (of a missionary subject, first part) to Coleridge, the most beautiful, cordial, and sincere.
10125{_}3663{_}He there acknowledges his obligation to S. T. C. for his knowledge of Gospel truths, the nature of a Christian Church, etc.,--to the talk of Samuel Taylor Coleridge (at whose Gamaliel feet he sits weekly), rather than to that of all the men living.
10125{_}3664{_}This from him, the great dandled and petted sectarian, to a religious character so equivocal in the world's eye as that of S. T. C., so foreign to the Kirk's estimate,--can this man be a quack?
10125{_}3665{_}The language is as affecting as the spirit of the dedication.
10125{_}3666{_}Some friend told him, "This dedication will do you no good,"--_i.
10125{_}3667{_}e._, not in the world's repute, or with your own people.
10125{_}3668{_}"That is a reason for doing it," quoth Irving.
10125{_}3669{_}I am thoroughly pleased with him.
10125{_}3670{_}He is firm, out-speaking, intrepid, and docile as a pupil of Pythagoras.
10125{_}3671{_}You must like him.
10125{_}3672{_}Yours, in tremors of painful hope,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3673{_}LXXXVI.
10125{_}3674{_}TO WORDSWORTH  _April_ 6, 1825  Dear Wordsworth,--I have been several times meditating a letter to you concerning the good thing which has befallen me; but the thought of poor Monkhouse [1] came across me.
10125{_}3675{_}He was one that I had exulted in the prospect of congratulating me.
10125{_}3676{_}He and you were to have been the first participators; for indeed it has been ten weeks since the first motion of it.
10125{_}3677{_}Here am I then, after thirty-three years' slavery, sitting in my own room at eleven o'clock this finest of all April mornings, a freed man, with L441 a year for the remainder of my life, live I as long as John Dennis, who outlived his annuity and starved at ninety: L441; _i.e., L450_, with a deduction of L9 for a provision secured to my sister, she being survivor, the pension guaranteed by Act Georgii Tertii, etc.
10125{_}3678{_}I came home FOREVER on Tuesday in last week.
10125{_}3679{_}The incomprehensibleness of my condition overwhelmed me; it was like passing from life into eternity.
10125{_}3680{_}Every year to be as long as three, _i.e._, to have three times as much real time--time that is my own--in it!
10125{_}3681{_}I wandered about thinking I was happy, but feeling I was not.
10125{_}3682{_}But that tumultuousness is passing off, and I begin to understand the nature of the gift.
10125{_}3683{_}Holidays, even the annual month, were always uneasy joys,--their conscious fugitiveness; the craving after making the most of them.
10125{_}3684{_}Now, when all is holiday, there are no holidays.
10125{_}3685{_}I can sit at home, in rain or shine, without a restless impulse for walkings.
10125{_}3686{_}I am daily steadying, and shall soon find it as natural to me to be my own master as it has been irksome to have had a master.
10125{_}3687{_}Mary wakes every morning with an obscure feeling that some good has happened to us.
10125{_}3688{_}Leigh Hunt and Montgomery, after their releasements, describe the shock of their emancipation much as I feel mine.
10125{_}3689{_}But it hurt their frames.
10125{_}3690{_}I eat, drink, and sleep sound as ever, I lay no anxious schemes for going hither and thither, but take things as they occur.
10125{_}3691{_}Yesterday I excursioned twenty miles; to-day I write a few letters.
10125{_}3692{_}Pleasuring was for fugitive play-days: mine are fugitive only in the sense that life is fugitive.
10125{_}3693{_}Freedom and life co-existent!
10125{_}3694{_}At the foot of such a call upon you for gratulation, I am ashamed to advert to that melancholy event.
10125{_}3695{_}Monkhouse was a character I learned to love slowly; but it grew upon me yearly, monthly, daily.
10125{_}3696{_}What a chasm has it made in our pleasant parties!
10125{_}3697{_}His noble, friendly face was always coming before me, till this hurrying event in my life came, and for the time has absorbed all interest; in fact, it has shaken me a little.
10125{_}3698{_}My old desk companions, with whom I have had such merry hours, seem to reproach me for removing my lot from among them.
10125{_}3699{_}They were pleasant creatures; but to the anxieties of business, and a weight of possible worse ever impending, I was not equal.
10125{_}3700{_}Tuthill and Gilman gave me my certificates; I laughed at the friendly lie implied in them.
10125{_}3701{_}But my sister shook her head, and said it was all true.
10125{_}3702{_}Indeed, this last winter I was jaded out; winters were always worse than other parts of the year, because the spirits are worse, and I had no daylight.
10125{_}3703{_}In summer I had daylight evenings.
10125{_}3704{_}The relief was hinted to me from a superior power when I, poor slave, had not a hope but that I must wait another seven years with Jacob; and lo!
10125{_}3705{_}the Rachel which I coveted is brought to me.
10125{_}3706{_}[1] Wordsworth's cousin, who was ill of consumption in Devonshire.
10125{_}3707{_}He died the following year.
10125{_}3708{_}LXXXVII.
10125{_}3709{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3710{_}_April_ 6, 1825.
10125{_}3711{_}Dear B.B.,--My spirits are so tumultuary with the novelty of my recent emancipation that I have scarce steadiness of hand, much more mind, to compose a letter.
10125{_}3712{_}I am free, B.B.,--free as air!
10125{_}3713{_}"The little bird that wings the sky   Knows no such liberty."
10125{_}3714{_}[1]   I was set free on Tuesday in last week at four   o'clock.
10125{_}3715{_}I came home forever!
10125{_}3716{_}I have been describing my feelings as well as I can to Wordsworth in a long letter, and don't care to repeat.
10125{_}3717{_}Take it, briefly, that for a few days I was painfully oppressed by so mighty a change; but it is becoming daily more natural to me.
10125{_}3718{_}I went and sat among 'em all at my old thirty-three-years' desk yester-morning; and, deuce take me, if I had not yearnings at leaving all my old pen-and-ink fellows, merry, sociable lads,--at leaving them in the lurch, fag, fag, fag!
10125{_}3719{_}The comparison of my own superior felicity gave me anything but pleasure.
10125{_}3720{_}B.B., I would not serve another seven years for seven hundred thousand pounds!
10125{_}3721{_}I have got L441 net for life, sanctioned by Act of Parliament, with a provision for Mary if she survives me.
10125{_}3722{_}I will live another fifty years; or if I live but ten, they will be thirty, reckoning the quantity of real time in them,--_i.e._, the time that is a man's own, Tell me how you like "Barbara S.;" [2] will it be received in atonement for the foolish "Vision"--I mean by the lady?
10125{_}3723{_}_A propos_, I never saw Mrs. Crawford in my life; nevertheless, it's all true of somebody.
10125{_}3724{_}Address me, in future, Colebrooke Cottage, Islington, I am really nervous (but that will wear off), so take this brief announcement.
10125{_}3725{_}Yours truly,  C.L.
10125{_}3726{_}[1] "The birds that wanton in the air     Know no such liberty."
10125{_}3727{_}LOVELACE.
10125{_}3728{_}[2] The Elia essay.
10125{_}3729{_}Fanny Kelly was the original of "Barbara S."    LXXXVIII.
10125{_}3730{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3731{_}_July_ 2, 1825.
10125{_}3732{_}I am hardly able to appreciate your volume now; [1] but I liked the dedication much, and the apology for your bald burying grounds.
10125{_}3733{_}To Shelley--but _that_ is not new, To the young Vesper-singer, Great Bealings, Playford, and what not.
10125{_}3734{_}If there be a cavil, it is that the topics of religious consolation, however beautiful, are repeated till a sort of triteness attends them.
10125{_}3735{_}It seems as if you were forever losing Friends' children by death, and reminding their parents of the Resurrection.
10125{_}3736{_}Do children die so often and so good in your parts?
10125{_}3737{_}The topic taken from the consideration that they are snatched away from _possible vanities_ seems hardly sound; for to an Omniscient eye their conditional failings must be one with their actual.
10125{_}3738{_}But I am too unwell for theology.
10125{_}3739{_}Such as I am,  I am yours and A.K.
10125{_}3740{_}'s truly,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3741{_}[1] "Barton's volume of Poems."
10125{_}3742{_}LXXXIX.
10125{_}3743{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3744{_}_August_ 10, 1825.
10125{_}3745{_}We shall be soon again at Colebrooke.
10125{_}3746{_}Dear B.B.,--You must excuse my not writing before, when I tell you we are on a visit at Enfield, where I do not feel it natural to sit down to a letter.
10125{_}3747{_}It is at all times an exertion.
10125{_}3748{_}I had rather talk with you and Anne Knight quietly at Colebrooke Lodge over the matter of your last.
10125{_}3749{_}You mistake me when you express misgivings about my relishing a series of Scriptural poems.
10125{_}3750{_}I wrote confusedly; what I meant to say was, that one or two consolatory poems on deaths would have had a more condensed effect than many.
10125{_}3751{_}Scriptural, devotional topics, admit of infinite variety.
10125{_}3752{_}So far from poetry tiring me because religious, I can read, and I say it seriously, the homely old version of the Psalms in our Prayer-books for an hour or two together sometimes, without sense of weariness.
10125{_}3753{_}I did not express myself clearly about what I think a false topic, insisted on so frequently in consolatory addresses on the death of infants.
10125{_}3754{_}I know something like it is in Scripture, but I think humanly spoken.
10125{_}3755{_}It is a natural thought, a sweet fallacy, to the survivors, but still a fallacy.
10125{_}3756{_}If it stands on the doctrine of this being a probationary state, it is liable to this dilemma.
10125{_}3757{_}Omniscience, to whom possibility must be clear as act, must know of the child what it would hereafter turn out: if good, then the topic is false to say it is secured from falling into future wilfulness, vice, etc.
10125{_}3758{_}If bad, I do not see how its exemption from certain future overt acts by being snatched away at all tells in its favor.
10125{_}3759{_}You stop the arm of a murderer, or arrest the finger of a pickpurse; but is not the guilt incurred as much by the intent as if never so much acted?
10125{_}3760{_}Why children are hurried off, and old reprobates of a hundred left, whose trial humanly we may think was complete at fifty, is among the obscurities of providence, The very notion of a state of probation has darkness in it.
10125{_}3761{_}The All-knower has no need of satisfying his eyes by seeing what we will do, when he knows before what we will do.
10125{_}3762{_}Methinks we might be condemned before commission.
10125{_}3763{_}In these things we grope and flounder; and if we can pick up a little human comfort that the child taken is snatched from vice (no great compliment to it, by the by), let us take it.
10125{_}3764{_}And as to where an untried child goes, whether to join the assembly of its elders who have borne the heat of the day,--fire-purified martyrs and torment-sifted confessors,--what know we?
10125{_}3765{_}We promise heaven, methinks, too cheaply, and assign large revenues to minors incompetent to manage them.
10125{_}3766{_}Epitaphs run upon this topic of consolation till the very frequency induces a cheapness.
10125{_}3767{_}Tickets for admission into paradise are sculptured out a penny a letter, twopence a syllable, etc.
10125{_}3768{_}It is all a mystery; and the more I try to express my meaning (having none that is clear), the more I flounder.
10125{_}3769{_}Finally, write what your own conscience, which to you is the unerring judge, deems best, and be careless about the whimsies of such a half-baked notionist as I am.
10125{_}3770{_}We are here in a most pleasant country, full of walks, and idle to our heart's desire.
10125{_}3771{_}Taylor has dropped the "London."
10125{_}3772{_}It was indeed a dead weight.
10125{_}3773{_}It had got in the Slough of Despond.
10125{_}3774{_}I shuffle off my part of the pack, and stand, like Christian, with light and merry shoulders.
10125{_}3775{_}It had got silly, indecorous, pert, and everything that is bad.
10125{_}3776{_}Both our kind _remembrances_ to Mrs. K. and yourself, and strangers'-greeting to Lucy,--is it Lucy, or Ruth?--that gathers wise sayings in a Book.
10125{_}3777{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}3778{_}XC.
10125{_}3779{_}TO SOUTHEY.
10125{_}3780{_}_August_ 19, 1825.
10125{_}3781{_}Dear Southey,--You'll know whom this letter comes from by opening slap-dash upon the text, as in the good old times.
10125{_}3782{_}I never could come into the custom of envelopes,--'tis a modern foppery; the Plinian correspondence gives no hint of such.
10125{_}3783{_}In singleness of sheet and meaning, then, I thank you for your little book.
10125{_}3784{_}I am ashamed to add a codicil of thanks for your "Book of the Church."
10125{_}3785{_}I scarce feel competent to give an opinion of the latter; I have not reading enough of that kind to venture at it.
10125{_}3786{_}I can only say the fact, that I have read it with attention and interest.
10125{_}3787{_}Being, as you know, not quite a Churchman, I felt a jealousy at the Church taking to herself the whole deserts of Christianity, Catholic and Protestant, from Druid extirpation downwards.
10125{_}3788{_}I call all good Christians the Church.
10125{_}3789{_}Capillarians and all.
10125{_}3790{_}But I am in too light a humor to touch these matters.
10125{_}3791{_}May all our churches flourish!
10125{_}3792{_}Two things staggered me in the poem (and one of them staggered both of as): I cannot away with a beautiful series of verses, as I protest they are, commencing "Jenner," 'Tis like a choice banquet opened with a pill or an electuary,--physic stuff.
10125{_}3793{_}T'other is, we cannot make out how Edith should be no more than ten years old.
10125{_}3794{_}By 'r Lady, we had taken her to be some sixteen or upwards.
10125{_}3795{_}We suppose you have only chosen the round number for the metre.
10125{_}3796{_}Or poem and dedication may be both older than they pretend to,--but then some hint might have been given; for, as it stands, it may only serve some day to puzzle the parish reckoning.
10125{_}3797{_}But without inquiring further (for 'tis ungracious to look into a lady's years), the dedication is eminently pleasing and tender, and we wish Edith May Southey joy of it.
10125{_}3798{_}Something, too, struck us as if we had heard of the death of John May.
10125{_}3799{_}A John May's death was a few years since in the papers.
10125{_}3800{_}We think the tale one of the quietest, prettiest things we have seen.
10125{_}3801{_}You have been temperate in the use of localities, which generally spoil poems laid in exotic regions.
10125{_}3802{_}You mostly cannot stir out (in such things) for humming-birds and fireflies.
10125{_}3803{_}A tree is a Magnolia, etc.--Can I but like the truly Catholic spirit?
10125{_}3804{_}"Blame as thou mayest the Papist's erring creed,"--which and other passages brought me back to the old Anthology days and the admonitory lesson to "Dear George" on "The Vesper Bell," a little poem which retains its first hold upon me strangely.
10125{_}3805{_}The compliment to the translatress is daintily conceived.
10125{_}3806{_}Nothing is choicer in that sort of writing than to bring in some remote, impossible parallel,--as between a great empress and the inobtrusive, quiet soul who digged her noiseless way so perseveringly through that rugged Paraguay mine.
10125{_}3807{_}How she Dobrizhoffered it all out, it puzzles my slender Latinity to conjecture.
10125{_}3808{_}Why do you seem to sanction Landor's unfeeling allegorizing away of honest Quixote?
10125{_}3809{_}He may as well say Strap is meant to symbolize the Scottish nation before the Union, and Random since that Act of dubious issue; or that Partridge means the Mystical Man, and Lady Bellaston typifies the Woman upon Many Waters.
10125{_}3810{_}Gebir, indeed, may mean the state of the hop markets last month, for anything I know to the contrary.
10125{_}3811{_}That all Spain overflowed with romancical books (as Madge Newcastle calls them) was no reason that Cervantes should not smile at the matter of them; nor even a reason that, in another mood, he might not multiply them, deeply as he was tinctured with the essence of them.
10125{_}3812{_}Quixote is the father of gentle ridicule, and at the same time the very depository and treasury of chivalry and highest notions.
10125{_}3813{_}Marry, when somebody persuaded Cervantes that he meant only fun, and put him upon writing that unfortunate Second Part, with the confederacies of that unworthy duke and most contemptible duchess, Cervantes sacrificed his instinct to his understanding.
10125{_}3814{_}We got your little book but last night, being at Enfield, to which place we came about a month since, and are having quiet holidays.
10125{_}3815{_}Mary walks her twelve miles a day some days, and I my twenty on others.
10125{_}3816{_}'T is all holiday with me now, you know; the change works admirably.
10125{_}3817{_}For literary news, in my poor way, I have a one-act farce [1] going to be acted at Haymarket; but when?
10125{_}3818{_}is the question, 'Tis an extravaganza, and like enough to follow "Mr. H." "The London Magazine" has shifted its publishers once more, and I shall shift myself out of it.
10125{_}3819{_}It is fallen.
10125{_}3820{_}My ambition is not at present higher than to write nonsense for the playhouses, to eke out a something contracted income.
10125{_}3821{_}_Tempus erat_.
10125{_}3822{_}There was a time, my dear Cornwallis, when the muse, etc.
10125{_}3823{_}But I am now in Mac Flecknoe's predicament,--  "Promised a play, and dwindled to a farce."
10125{_}3824{_}Coleridge is better (was, at least, a few weeks since) than he has been for years.
10125{_}3825{_}His accomplishing his book at last has been a source of vigor to him.
10125{_}3826{_}We are on a half visit to his friend Allsop, at a Mrs. Leishman's, Enfield, but expect to be at Colebrooke Cottage in a week or so, where, or anywhere, I shall be always most happy to receive tidings from you.
10125{_}3827{_}G. Dyer is in the height of an uxorious paradise.
10125{_}3828{_}His honeymoon will not wane till he wax cold.
10125{_}3829{_}Never was a more happy pair, since Acme and Septimius, and longer.
10125{_}3830{_}Farewell, with many thanks, dear S. Our loves to all round your Wrekin.
10125{_}3831{_}Your old friend,  C. LAMB.
10125{_}3832{_}[1] Probably "The Pawnbroker's Daughter," which happily was not destined to be performed.--AINGER.
10125{_}3833{_}XCI.
10125{_}3834{_}TO BERNARD BARTON.
10125{_}3835{_}_March_ 20, 1826.
10125{_}3836{_}Dear B.
10125{_}3837{_}B.,--You may know my letters by the paper and the folding.
10125{_}3838{_}For the former, I live on scraps obtained in charity from an old friend, whose stationery is a permanent perquisite; for folding, I shall do it neatly when I learn to tie my neckcloths.
10125{_}3839{_}I surprise most of my friends by writing to them on ruled paper, as if I had not got past pothooks and hangers.
10125{_}3840{_}Sealing-wax I have none on my establishment; wafers of the coarsest bran supply its place.
10125{_}3841{_}When my epistles come to be weighed with Pliny's, however superior to the Roman in delicate irony, judicious reflections, etc., his gilt post will bribe over the judges to him.
10125{_}3842{_}All the time I was at the E. I. H. I never mended a pen; I now cut 'em to the stumps, marring rather than mending the primitive goose-quill.
10125{_}3843{_}I cannot bear to pay for articles I used to get for nothing.
10125{_}3844{_}When Adam laid out his first penny upon nonpareils at some stall in Mesopotamos, I think it went hard with him, reflecting upon his old goodly orchard, where he had so many for nothing.
10125{_}3845{_}When I write to a great man at the court end, he opens with surprise upon a naked note, such as Whitechapel people interchange, with no sweet degrees of envelope.
10125{_}3846{_}I never enclosed one bit of paper in another, nor understood the rationale of it.
10125{_}3847{_}Once only I sealed with borrowed wax, to set Walter Scott a-wondering, signed with the imperial quartered arms of England, which my friend Field bears in compliment to his descent, in the female line, from Oliver Cromwell.
10125{_}3848{_}It must have set his antiquarian curiosity upon watering.
10125{_}3849{_}To your questions upon the currency, I refer you to Mr. Robinson's last speech, where, if you can find a solution, I cannot.
10125{_}3850{_}I think this, though,--the best ministry we ever stumbled upon,--gin reduced four shillings in the gallon, wine two shillings in the quart!
10125{_}3851{_}This comes home to men's minds and bosoms.
10125{_}3852{_}My tirade against visitors was not meant _particularly_ at you or Anne Knight.
10125{_}3853{_}I scarce know what I meant, for I do not just now feel the grievance.
10125{_}3854{_}I wanted to make an _article_.
10125{_}3855{_}So in another thing I talked of somebody's _insipid wife_ without a correspondent object in my head; and a good lady, a friend's wife, whom I really _love_ (don't startle, I mean in a licit way), has looked shyly on me ever since.
10125{_}3856{_}The blunders of personal application are ludicrous.
10125{_}3857{_}I send out a character every now and then on purpose to exercise the ingenuity of my friends.
10125{_}3858{_}"Popular Fallacies" will go on; that word "concluded" is an erratum, I suppose, for "continued."
10125{_}3859{_}I do not know how it got stuffed in there.
10125{_}3860{_}A little thing without name will also be printed on the Religion of the Actors; but it is out of your way, so I recommend you, with true author's hypocrisy, to skip it.
10125{_}3861{_}We are about to sit down to roast beef, at which we could wish A. K., B.
10125{_}3862{_}B., and B.
10125{_}3863{_}B.
10125{_}3864{_}'s pleasant daughter to be humble partakers.
10125{_}3865{_}So much for my hint at visitors, which was scarcely calculated for droppers-in from Woodbridge; the sky does not drop such larks every day.
10125{_}3866{_}My very kindest wishes to you all three, with my sister's best love.
10125{_}3867{_}C. LAMB.
10125{_}3868{_}XCII.
10125{_}3869{_}TO J.
10125{_}3870{_}B. DIBDIN.
10125{_}3871{_}_June_, 1826.
10125{_}3872{_}Dear D.,--My first impulse upon seeing your letter was pleasure at seeing your old neat hand, nine parts gentlemanly, with a modest dash of the clerical; my second, a thought natural enough this hot weather: Am I to answer all this?
10125{_}3873{_}Why, 't is as long as those to the Ephesians and Galatians put together: I have counted the words, for curiosity....
10125{_}3874{_}I never knew an enemy to puns who was not an ill-natured man.
10125{_}3875{_}Your fair critic in the coach reminds me of a Scotchman, who assured me he did not see much in Shakspeare.
10125{_}3876{_}I replied, I daresay _not_.
10125{_}3877{_}He felt the equivoke, looked awkward and reddish, but soon returned to the attack by saying that he thought Burns was as good as Shakspeare.
10125{_}3878{_}I said that I had no doubt he was,--to a _Scotchman_.
10125{_}3879{_}We exchanged no more words that day.... Let me hear that you have clambered up to Lover's Seat; it is as fine in that neighborhood as Juan Fernandez,--as lonely, too, when the fishing-boats are not out; I have sat for hours staring upon a shipless sea.
10125{_}3880{_}The salt sea is never as grand as when it is left to itself.
10125{_}3881{_}One cock-boat spoils it; a seamew or two improves it.
10125{_}3882{_}And go to the little church, which is a very Protestant Loretto, and seems dropped by some angel for the use of a hermit who was at once parishioner and a whole parish.
10125{_}3883{_}It is not too big.
10125{_}3884{_}Go in the night, bring it away in your portmanteau, and I will plant it in my garden.
10125{_}3885{_}It must have been erected, in the very infancy of British Christianity, for the two or three first converts, yet with all the appurtenances of a church of the first magnitude,--its pulpit, its pews, its baptismal font; a cathedral in a nutshell.
10125{_}3886{_}The minister that divides the Word there must give lumping pennyworths.
10125{_}3887{_}It is built to the text of "two or three assembled in my name."
10125{_}3888{_}It reminds me of the grain of mustard-seed.
10125{_}3889{_}If the glebe land is proportionate, it may yield two potatoes.
10125{_}3890{_}Tithes out of it could be no more split than a hair.
10125{_}3891{_}Its First fruits must be its Last, for 't would never produce a couple.
10125{_}3892{_}It is truly the strait and narrow way, and few there be (of London visitants) that find it.
10125{_}3893{_}The still small voice is surely to be found there, if anywhere.
10125{_}3894{_}A sounding-board is merely there for ceremony.
10125{_}3895{_}It is secure from earthquakes, not more from sanctity than size, for't would feel a mountain thrown upon it no more than a taper-worm would.
